<dt class=""glossary"" id=""abel&#39;s_test"" style=""margin-top: 0.4em;""><dfn class=""glossary""><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abel%27s_test"" title=""Abel&#39;s test"">Abel's test</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class=""glossary"">A method of testing for the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Convergent_series"" title=""Convergent series"">convergence</a> of an <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Series_(mathematics)"" title=""Series (mathematics)"">infinite series</a>.</dd>
<dt class=""glossary"" id=""absolute_convergence"" style=""margin-top: 0.4em;""><dfn class=""glossary""><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Absolute_convergence"" title=""Absolute convergence"">Absolute convergence</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class=""glossary"">An <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Series_(mathematics)"" title=""Series (mathematics)"">infinite series</a> of numbers is said to <b>converge absolutely</b> (or to be <b>absolutely convergent</b>) if the sum of the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Absolute_value"" title=""Absolute value"">absolute values</a> of the summands is finite. More precisely, a real or complex series <span class=""mwe-math-element""><span class=""mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y"" style=""display: none;""><math xmlns=""http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML""  alttext=""{\displaystyle \textstyle \sum _{n=0}^{\infty }a_{n}}"">

<dt class=""glossary"" id=""absolute_maximum"" style=""margin-top: 0.4em;""><dfn class=""glossary""><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Maxima_and_minima"" title=""Maxima and minima"">Absolute maximum</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class=""glossary""></dd>
<dt class=""glossary"" id=""absolute_minimum"" style=""margin-top: 0.4em;""><dfn class=""glossary""><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Maxima_and_minima"" title=""Maxima and minima"">Absolute minimum</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class=""glossary""></dd>
<dt class=""glossary"" id=""absolute_value"" style=""margin-top: 0.4em;""><dfn class=""glossary""><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Absolute_value"" title=""Absolute value"">Absolute value</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class=""glossary"">The <b>absolute value</b> or <b>modulus</b> <span class=""texhtml"">|<i>x</i>|</span> of a <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Real_number"" title=""Real number"">real number</a>&#160;<span class=""texhtml mvar"" style=""font-style:italic;"">x</span> is the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Non-negative"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Non-negative"">non-negative</a> value of&#160;<span class=""texhtml mvar"" style=""font-style:italic;"">x</span> without regard to its <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sign_(mathematics)"" title=""Sign (mathematics)"">sign</a>. Namely, <span class=""texhtml"">|<i>x</i>| = <i>x</i></span> for a <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Positive_number"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Positive number"">positive</a>&#160;<span class=""texhtml mvar"" style=""font-style:italic;"">x</span>, <span class=""texhtml"">|<i>x</i>| = −<i>x</i></span> for a <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Negative_number"" title=""Negative number"">negative</a>&#160;<span class=""texhtml mvar"" style=""font-style:italic;"">x</span> (in which case <span class=""texhtml"">−<i>x</i></span> is positive), and <span class=""texhtml"">|0| = 0</span>. For example, the absolute value of 3 is 3, and the absolute value of −3 is also 3. The absolute value of a number may be thought of as its <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Distance"" title=""Distance"">distance</a> from zero.</dd>
<dt class=""glossary"" id=""alternating_series"" style=""margin-top: 0.4em;""><dfn class=""glossary""><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alternating_series"" title=""Alternating series"">Alternating series</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class=""glossary"">An <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Infinite_series"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Infinite series"">infinite series</a> whose terms alternate between positive and negative.</dd>
<dt class=""glossary"" id=""alternating_series_test"" style=""margin-top: 0.4em;""><dfn class=""glossary""><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alternating_series_test"" title=""Alternating series test"">Alternating series test</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class=""glossary"">Is the method used to prove that an <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alternating_series"" title=""Alternating series"">alternating series</a> with terms that decrease in absolute value is a <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Convergent_series"" title=""Convergent series"">convergent series</a>. The test was used by <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gottfried_Leibniz"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Gottfried Leibniz"">Gottfried Leibniz</a> and is sometimes known as <b>Leibniz's test</b>, <b>Leibniz's rule</b>, or the <b>Leibniz criterion</b>.</dd>
<dt class=""glossary"" id=""annulus"" style=""margin-top: 0.4em;""><dfn class=""glossary""><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Annulus_(mathematics)"" title=""Annulus (mathematics)"">Annulus</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class=""glossary"">A ring-shaped object, a region bounded by two <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Concentric_circles"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Concentric circles"">concentric circles</a>.</dd>
<dt class=""glossary"" id=""antiderivative"" style=""margin-top: 0.4em;""><dfn class=""glossary""><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Antiderivative"" title=""Antiderivative"">Antiderivative</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class=""glossary"">An <b>antiderivative</b>, <b>primitive function</b>, <b>primitive integral</b> or <b>indefinite integral</b><sup id=""cite_ref-1"" class=""reference""><a href=""#cite_note-1"">&#91;Note 1&#93;</a></sup> of a <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Function_(mathematics)"" title=""Function (mathematics)"">function</a> <span class=""texhtml""><i>f</i></span> is a differentiable function <span class=""texhtml""><i>F</i></span> whose <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Derivative"" title=""Derivative"">derivative</a> is equal to the original function <span class=""texhtml""><i>f</i></span>. This can be stated symbolically as <span class=""mwe-math-element""><span class=""mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y"" style=""display: none;""><math xmlns=""http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML""  alttext=""{\displaystyle F'=f}"">
  <semantics>
    <mrow class=""MJX-TeXAtom-ORD"">
      <mstyle displaystyle=""true"" scriptlevel=""0"">
        <msup>
          <mi>F</mi>
          <mo>&#x2032;</mo>
        </msup>
        <mo>=</mo>
        <mi>f</mi>
      </mstyle>
    </mrow>
    <annotation encoding=""application/x-tex"">{\displaystyle F'=f}</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math></span><img src=""https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/c10131b70a7b0b4c39667f5386b14c449a5217e7"" class=""mwe-math-fallback-image-inline"" aria-hidden=""true"" style=""vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:6.877ex; height:2.843ex;"" alt=""{\displaystyle F&#039;=f}""/></span>.<sup id=""cite_ref-2"" class=""reference""><a href=""#cite_note-2"">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id=""cite_ref-3"" class=""reference""><a href=""#cite_note-3"">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> The process of solving for antiderivatives is called <b>antidifferentiation</b> (or <b>indefinite integration</b>) and its opposite operation is called differentiation, which is the process of finding a derivative.</dd>
<dt class=""glossary"" id=""arcsin"" style=""margin-top: 0.4em;""><dfn class=""glossary""><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Inverse_trigonometric_functions"" title=""Inverse trigonometric functions"">Arcsin</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class=""glossary""></dd>
<dt class=""glossary"" id=""area_under_a_curve"" style=""margin-top: 0.4em;""><dfn class=""glossary""><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Area_under_a_curve"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Area under a curve"">Area under a curve</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class=""glossary""></dd>
<dt class=""glossary"" id=""asymptote"" style=""margin-top: 0.4em;""><dfn class=""glossary""><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Asymptote"" title=""Asymptote"">Asymptote</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class=""glossary"">In <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Analytic_geometry"" title=""Analytic geometry"">analytic geometry</a>, an <b>asymptote</b> of a <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Curve"" title=""Curve"">curve</a> is a line such that the distance between the curve and the line approaches zero as one or both of the <i>x</i> or <i>y</i> coordinates <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Limit_of_a_function#Limits_at_infinity"" title=""Limit of a function"">tends to infinity</a>. Some sources include the requirement that the curve may not cross the line infinitely often, but this is unusual for modern authors.<sup id=""cite_ref-4"" class=""reference""><a href=""#cite_note-4"">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> In <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Projective_geometry"" title=""Projective geometry"">projective geometry</a> and related contexts, an asymptote of a curve is a line which is <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tangent"" title=""Tangent"">tangent</a> to the curve at a <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Point_at_infinity"" title=""Point at infinity"">point at infinity</a>.<sup id=""cite_ref-5"" class=""reference""><a href=""#cite_note-5"">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id=""cite_ref-6"" class=""reference""><a href=""#cite_note-6"">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup></dd>
<dt class=""glossary"" id=""automatic_differentiation"" style=""margin-top: 0.4em;""><dfn class=""glossary""><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Automatic_differentiation"" title=""Automatic differentiation"">Automatic differentiation</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class=""glossary"">In <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mathematics"" title=""Mathematics"">mathematics</a> and <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Computer_algebra"" title=""Computer algebra"">computer algebra</a>, <b>automatic differentiation</b> (<b>AD</b>), also called <b>algorithmic differentiation</b> or <b>computational differentiation</b>,<sup id=""cite_ref-7"" class=""reference""><a href=""#cite_note-7"">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id=""cite_ref-baydin2018automatic_8-0"" class=""reference""><a href=""#cite_note-baydin2018automatic-8"">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> is a set of techniques to numerically evaluate the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Derivative"" title=""Derivative"">derivative</a> of a function specified by a computer program. AD exploits the fact that every computer program, no matter how complicated, executes a sequence of elementary arithmetic operations (addition, subtraction, multiplication, division, etc.) and elementary functions (exp, log, sin, cos, etc.). By applying the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chain_rule"" title=""Chain rule"">chain rule</a> repeatedly to these operations, derivatives of arbitrary order can be computed automatically, accurately to working precision, and using at most a small constant factor more arithmetic operations than the original program.</dd>

<dt class=""glossary"" id=""average_rate_of_change"" style=""margin-top: 0.4em;""><dfn class=""glossary""><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rate_(mathematics)#rate_of_change"" title=""Rate (mathematics)"">Average rate of change</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class=""glossary""></dd>
<dt class=""glossary"" id=""binomial_coefficient"" style=""margin-top: 0.4em;""><dfn class=""glossary""><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Binomial_coefficient"" title=""Binomial coefficient"">Binomial coefficient</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class=""glossary"">Any of the positive <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Integer"" title=""Integer"">integers</a> that occurs as a <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Coefficient"" title=""Coefficient"">coefficient</a> in the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Binomial_theorem"" title=""Binomial theorem"">binomial theorem</a> is a <b>binomial coefficient</b>. Commonly, a binomial coefficient is indexed by a pair of integers <span class=""texhtml""><i>n</i> ≥ <i>k</i> ≥ 0</span> and is written <span class=""mwe-math-element""><span class=""mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y"" style=""display: none;""><math xmlns=""http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML""  alttext=""{\displaystyle {\tbinom {n}{k}}.}"">

</math></span><img src=""https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/a2457a7ef3c77831e34e06a1fe17a80b84a03181"" class=""mwe-math-fallback-image-inline"" aria-hidden=""true"" style=""vertical-align: -2.671ex; width:19.158ex; height:6.343ex;"" alt=""{\displaystyle {\binom {n}{k}}={\frac {n!}{k!(n-k)!}}.}""/></span></dd></dl></dd></dl>
<dt class=""glossary"" id=""binomial_theorem&#39;&#39;&#39;_(or_&#39;&#39;&#39;binomial_expansion)"" style=""margin-top: 0.4em;""><dfn class=""glossary""><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Binomial_theorem"" title=""Binomial theorem"">Binomial theorem</a><b> (or </b><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Binomial_expansion"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Binomial expansion"">binomial expansion</a>)</dfn></dt>
<dd class=""glossary""> Describes the algebraic expansion of <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Exponentiation"" title=""Exponentiation"">powers</a> of a <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Binomial_(polynomial)"" title=""Binomial (polynomial)"">binomial</a>.</dd>
<dt class=""glossary"" id=""bounded_function"" style=""margin-top: 0.4em;""><dfn class=""glossary""><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bounded_function"" title=""Bounded function"">Bounded function</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class=""glossary"">A <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Function_(mathematics)"" title=""Function (mathematics)"">function</a> <i>f</i> defined on some <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Set_(mathematics)"" title=""Set (mathematics)"">set</a> <i>X</i> with <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Real_number"" title=""Real number"">real</a> or <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Complex_number"" title=""Complex number"">complex</a> values is called <b>bounded</b>, if the set of its values is <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bounded_set"" title=""Bounded set"">bounded</a>. In other words, <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/There_exists"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""There exists"">there exists</a> a real number <i>M</i> such that
<dl><dd><span class=""mwe-math-element""><span class=""mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y"" style=""display: none;""><math xmlns=""http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML""  alttext=""{\displaystyle |f(x)|\leq M}"">
  <semantics>
    <mrow class=""MJX-TeXAtom-ORD"">
      <mstyle displaystyle=""true"" scriptlevel=""0"">
        <mrow class=""MJX-TeXAtom-ORD"">
          <mo stretchy=""false"">|</mo>
        </mrow>
        <mi>f</mi>
        <mo stretchy=""false"">(</mo>
        <mi>x</mi>
        <mo stretchy=""false"">)</mo>
        <mrow class=""MJX-TeXAtom-ORD"">
          <mo stretchy=""false"">|</mo>
        </mrow>
        <mo>&#x2264;<!-- ≤ --></mo>
        <mi>M</mi>
      </mstyle>
    </mrow>
    <annotation encoding=""application/x-tex"">{\displaystyle |f(x)|\leq M}</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math></span><img src=""https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/bfc8ec4d3c57aa126bee5459febb9832b99cbcc5"" class=""mwe-math-fallback-image-inline"" aria-hidden=""true"" style=""vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:11.252ex; height:2.843ex;"" alt=""|f(x)|\le M""/></span></dd></dl>
<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/For_all"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""For all"">for all</a> <i>x</i> in <i>X</i>. A function that is <i>not</i> bounded is said to be <b>unbounded</b>.

Sometimes, if <i>f</i>(<i>x</i>) ≤ <i>A</i> for all <i>x</i> in <i>X</i>, then the function is said to be <b>bounded above</b> by <i>A</i>. On the other hand, if <i>f</i>(<i>x</i>) ≥ <i>B</i> for all <i>x</i> in <i>X</i>, then the function is said to be <b>bounded below</b> by <i>B</i>.</dd>
<dt class=""glossary"" id=""bounded_sequence"" style=""margin-top: 0.4em;""><dfn class=""glossary""><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bounded_function#bounded_sequence"" title=""Bounded function"">Bounded sequence</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class=""glossary""> .</dd>
<dt class=""glossary"" id=""calculus"" style=""margin-top: 0.4em;""><dfn class=""glossary""><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Calculus"" title=""Calculus"">Calculus</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class=""glossary"">(From <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Latin"" title=""Latin"">Latin</a> <i>calculus</i>, literally 'small pebble', used for counting and calculations, as on an <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abacus"" title=""Abacus"">abacus</a>)<sup id=""cite_ref-oxdic_9-0"" class=""reference""><a href=""#cite_note-oxdic-9"">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> is the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mathematics"" title=""Mathematics"">mathematical</a> study of continuous change, in the same way that <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Geometry"" title=""Geometry"">geometry</a> is the study of shape and <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Algebra"" title=""Algebra"">algebra</a> is the study of generalizations of <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arithmetic_operations"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Arithmetic operations"">arithmetic operations</a>.</dd>
<dt class=""glossary"" id=""cavalieri&#39;s_principle"" style=""margin-top: 0.4em;""><dfn class=""glossary""><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cavalieri%27s_principle"" title=""Cavalieri&#39;s principle"">Cavalieri's principle</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class=""glossary""> <b>Cavalieri's principle</b>, a modern implementation of the <b>method of indivisibles</b>, named after <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bonaventura_Cavalieri"" title=""Bonaventura Cavalieri"">Bonaventura Cavalieri</a>, is as follows:<sup id=""cite_ref-10"" class=""reference""><a href=""#cite_note-10"">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>
<ul><li><b>2-dimensional case</b>: Suppose two regions in a plane are included between two parallel lines in that plane. If every line parallel to these two lines intersects both regions in line segments of equal length, then the two regions have equal areas.</li>
<li><b>3-dimensional case</b>: Suppose two regions in three-space (solids) are included between two parallel planes. If every plane parallel to these two planes intersects both regions in <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cross_section_(geometry)"" title=""Cross section (geometry)"">cross-sections</a> of equal area, then the two regions have equal volumes.</li></ul></dd>
<dt class=""glossary"" id=""chain_rule"" style=""margin-top: 0.4em;""><dfn class=""glossary""><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chain_rule"" title=""Chain rule"">Chain rule</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class=""glossary"">The <b>chain rule</b> is a <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Formula"" title=""Formula"">formula</a> for computing the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Derivative"" title=""Derivative"">derivative</a> of the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Function_composition"" title=""Function composition"">composition</a> of two or more <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Function_(mathematics)"" title=""Function (mathematics)"">functions</a>. That is, if <i>f</i> and <i>g</i> are functions, then the chain rule expresses the derivative of their composition <span class=""texhtml""><i>f</i> <span style=""font-size:large;"">∘</span> <i>g</i></span> (the function which maps <i>x</i> to <i>f</i>(<i>g</i>(<i>x</i>)) ) in terms of the derivatives of <i>f</i> and <i>g</i> and the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pointwise_product"" title=""Pointwise product"">product of functions</a> as follows:

<dl><dd><span class=""mwe-math-element""><span class=""mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y"" style=""display: none;""><math xmlns=""http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML""  alttext=""{\displaystyle (f\circ g)'=(f'\circ g)\cdot g'.}"">
  <semantics>
    <mrow class=""MJX-TeXAtom-ORD"">
      <mstyle displaystyle=""true"" scriptlevel=""0"">
        <mo stretchy=""false"">(</mo>

        <mi>g</mi>
        <msup>
          <mo stretchy=""false"">)</mo>
          <mo>&#x2032;</mo>
        </msup>
        <mo>=</mo>
        <mo stretchy=""false"">(</mo>
        <msup>
          <mi>f</mi>
          <mo>&#x2032;</mo>
        </msup>
        <mo>&#x2218;<!-- ∘ --></mo>
        <mi>g</mi>
        <mo stretchy=""false"">)</mo>
        <mo>&#x22C5;<!-- ⋅ --></mo>
        <msup>
          <mi>g</mi>
          <mo>&#x2032;</mo>
        </msup>
        <mo>.</mo>
      </mstyle>
    </mrow>
    <annotation encoding=""application/x-tex"">{\displaystyle (f\circ g)'=(f'\circ g)\cdot g'.}</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math></span><img src=""https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/fb55cd5448d4bed6da3b79283d92eec2ab9bb95d"" class=""mwe-math-fallback-image-inline"" aria-hidden=""true"" style=""vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:21.436ex; height:3.009ex;"" alt=""(f\circ g)&#039;=(f&#039;\circ g)\cdot g&#039;.""/></span></dd></dl>

This may equivalently be expressed in terms of the variable. Let <span class=""texhtml""><i>F</i> = <i>f</i> <span style=""font-size:large;"">∘</span> <i>g</i></span>, or equivalently, <span class=""texhtml""><i>F</i>(<i>x</i>) = <i>f</i>(<i>g</i>(<i>x</i>))</span> for all <i>x</i>. Then one can also write
<dl><dd><span class=""mwe-math-element""><span class=""mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y"" style=""display: none;""><math xmlns=""http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML""  alttext=""{\displaystyle F'(x)=f'(g(x))g'(x).}"">

          <mi>f</mi>
          <mo>&#x2032;</mo>
        </msup>
        <mo stretchy=""false"">(</mo>
        <mi>g</mi>
        <mo stretchy=""false"">(</mo>
        <mi>x</mi>
        <mo stretchy=""false"">)</mo>
        <mo stretchy=""false"">)</mo>
        <msup>
          <mi>g</mi>
          <mo>&#x2032;</mo>
        </msup>
        <mo stretchy=""false"">(</mo>
        <mi>x</mi>

    <annotation encoding=""application/x-tex"">{\displaystyle F'(x)=f'(g(x))g'(x).}</annotation>

</math></span><img src=""https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/63b54293b9ca115caa553c556cc7faa6a022888a"" class=""mwe-math-fallback-image-inline"" aria-hidden=""true"" style=""vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:22.395ex; height:3.009ex;"" alt=""F&#039;(x)=f&#039;(g(x))g&#039;(x).""/></span></dd></dl>

The chain rule may be written in <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Leibniz%27s_notation"" title=""Leibniz&#39;s notation"">Leibniz's notation</a> in the following way. If a variable <i>z</i> depends on the variable <i>y</i>, which itself depends on the variable <i>x</i>, so that <i>y</i> and <i>z</i> are therefore <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dependent_variable"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Dependent variable"">dependent variables</a>, then <i>z</i>, via the intermediate variable of <i>y</i>, depends on <i>x</i> as well. The chain rule then states,

The two versions of the chain rule are related; if <span class=""mwe-math-element""><span class=""mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y"" style=""display: none;""><math xmlns=""http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML""  alttext=""{\displaystyle z=f(y)}"">

<dl><dd><span class=""mwe-math-element""><span class=""mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y"" style=""display: none;""><math xmlns=""http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML""  alttext=""{\displaystyle {\frac {dz}{dx}}={\frac {dz}{dy}}\cdot {\frac {dy}{dx}}=f'(y)g'(x)=f'(g(x))g'(x).}"">

In <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Integral"" title=""Integral"">integration</a>, the counterpart to the chain rule is the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Substitution_rule"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Substitution rule"">substitution rule</a>.</dd>
<dt class=""glossary"" id=""change_of_variables"" style=""margin-top: 0.4em;""><dfn class=""glossary""><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Change_of_variables"" title=""Change of variables"">Change of variables</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class=""glossary""> Is a basic technique used to simplify problems in which the original <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Variable_(mathematics)"" title=""Variable (mathematics)"">variables</a> are replaced with <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Function_(mathematics)"" title=""Function (mathematics)"">functions</a> of other variables. The intent is that when expressed in new variables, the problem may become simpler, or equivalent to a better understood problem.</dd>
<dt class=""glossary"" id=""cofunction"" style=""margin-top: 0.4em;""><dfn class=""glossary""><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cofunction"" title=""Cofunction"">Cofunction</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class=""glossary"">A <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Function_(mathematics)"" title=""Function (mathematics)"">function</a> <i>f</i> is <b>cofunction</b> of a function <i>g</i> if <i>f</i>(<i>A</i>) = <i>g</i>(<i>B</i>) whenever <i>A</i> and <i>B</i> are <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Complementary_angles"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Complementary angles"">complementary angles</a>.<sup id=""cite_ref-Hall_1909_11-0"" class=""reference""><a href=""#cite_note-Hall_1909-11"">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> This definition typically applies to <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Trigonometric_functions"" title=""Trigonometric functions"">trigonometric functions</a>.<sup id=""cite_ref-Aufmann_Nation_2014_12-0"" class=""reference""><a href=""#cite_note-Aufmann_Nation_2014-12"">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id=""cite_ref-Bales_2012_13-0"" class=""reference""><a href=""#cite_note-Bales_2012-13"">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> The prefix ""co-"" can be found already in <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Edmund_Gunter"" title=""Edmund Gunter"">Edmund Gunter</a>'s <i>Canon triangulorum</i> (1620).<sup id=""cite_ref-Gunter_1620_14-0"" class=""reference""><a href=""#cite_note-Gunter_1620-14"">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup><sup id=""cite_ref-Roegel_2010_15-0"" class=""reference""><a href=""#cite_note-Roegel_2010-15"">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> .</dd>
<dt class=""glossary"" id=""concave_function"" style=""margin-top: 0.4em;""><dfn class=""glossary""><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Concave_function"" title=""Concave function"">Concave function</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class=""glossary""> Is the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Additive_inverse"" title=""Additive inverse"">negative</a> of a <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Convex_function"" title=""Convex function"">convex function</a>. A concave function is also <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Synonym"" title=""Synonym"">synonymously</a> called <b>concave downwards</b>, <b>concave down</b>, <b>convex upwards</b>, <b>convex cap</b> or <b>upper convex</b>.</dd>
<dt class=""glossary"" id=""constant_of_integration"" style=""margin-top: 0.4em;""><dfn class=""glossary""><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Constant_of_integration"" title=""Constant of integration"">Constant of integration</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class=""glossary"">The <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Indefinite_integral"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Indefinite integral"">indefinite integral</a> of a given function (i.e., the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Set_(mathematics)"" title=""Set (mathematics)"">set</a> of all <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Antiderivative"" title=""Antiderivative"">antiderivatives</a> of the function) on a <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Connected_set"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Connected set"">connected domain</a> is only defined <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Up_to"" title=""Up to"">up to</a> an additive constant, the <b>constant of integration</b>.<sup id=""cite_ref-16"" class=""reference""><a href=""#cite_note-16"">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup><sup id=""cite_ref-17"" class=""reference""><a href=""#cite_note-17"">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> This constant expresses an ambiguity inherent in the construction of antiderivatives. If a function <span class=""mwe-math-element""><span class=""mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y"" style=""display: none;""><math xmlns=""http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML""  alttext=""{\displaystyle f(x)}"">

The constant of integration is sometimes omitted in <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lists_of_integrals"" title=""Lists of integrals"">lists of integrals</a> for simplicity.</dd>
<dt class=""glossary"" id=""continuous_function"" style=""margin-top: 0.4em;""><dfn class=""glossary""><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Continuous_function"" title=""Continuous function"">Continuous function</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class=""glossary"">Is a <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Function_(mathematics)"" title=""Function (mathematics)"">function</a> for which sufficiently small changes in the input result in arbitrarily small changes in the output. Otherwise, a function is said to be a <i>discontinuous</i> function. A continuous function with a continuous <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Inverse_function"" title=""Inverse function"">inverse function</a> is called a <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Homeomorphism"" title=""Homeomorphism"">homeomorphism</a>.</dd>
<dt class=""glossary"" id=""continuously_differentiable"" style=""margin-top: 0.4em;""><dfn class=""glossary""><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Differentiable_function#Differentiability_classes"" title=""Differentiable function"">Continuously differentiable</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class=""glossary"">A function <i>f</i> is said to be <i>continuously differentiable</i> if the derivative <i>f<span class=""nowrap"" style=""padding-left:0.15em;"">′</span></i>(<i>x</i>) exists and is itself a continuous function.</dd>
<dt class=""glossary"" id=""contour_integration"" style=""margin-top: 0.4em;""><dfn class=""glossary""><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Methods_of_contour_integration"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Methods of contour integration"">Contour integration</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class=""glossary"">In the mathematical field of <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Complex_analysis"" title=""Complex analysis"">complex analysis</a>, <b>contour integration</b> is a method of evaluating certain <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Integral"" title=""Integral"">integrals</a> along paths in the complex plane.<sup id=""cite_ref-Stalker_18-0"" class=""reference""><a href=""#cite_note-Stalker-18"">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup><sup id=""cite_ref-Bak_19-0"" class=""reference""><a href=""#cite_note-Bak-19"">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup><sup id=""cite_ref-Krantz_20-0"" class=""reference""><a href=""#cite_note-Krantz-20"">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup></dd>
<dt class=""glossary"" id=""convergence_tests"" style=""margin-top: 0.4em;""><dfn class=""glossary""><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Convergence_test"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Convergence test"">Convergence tests</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class=""glossary"">Are methods of testing for the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Convergent_series"" title=""Convergent series"">convergence</a>, <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Conditional_convergence"" title=""Conditional convergence"">conditional convergence</a>, <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Absolute_convergence"" title=""Absolute convergence"">absolute convergence</a>, <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Interval_of_convergence"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Interval of convergence"">interval of convergence</a> or divergence of an <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Series_(mathematics)"" title=""Series (mathematics)"">infinite series</a> <span class=""mwe-math-element""><span class=""mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y"" style=""display: none;""><math xmlns=""http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML""  alttext=""{\displaystyle \sum _{n=1}^{\infty }a_{n}}"">

<dt class=""glossary"" id=""convergent_series"" style=""margin-top: 0.4em;""><dfn class=""glossary""><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Convergent_series"" title=""Convergent series"">Convergent series</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class=""glossary"">In <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mathematics"" title=""Mathematics"">mathematics</a>, a <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Series_(mathematics)"" title=""Series (mathematics)"">series</a> is the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Summation"" title=""Summation"">sum</a> of the terms of an <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Infinite_sequence"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Infinite sequence"">infinite sequence</a> of numbers.

Given an infinite sequence <span class=""mwe-math-element""><span class=""mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y"" style=""display: none;""><math xmlns=""http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML""  alttext=""{\displaystyle \left(a_{1},\ a_{2},\ a_{3},\dots \right)}"">

A series is <b>convergent</b> if the sequence of its partial sums <span class=""mwe-math-element""><span class=""mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y"" style=""display: none;""><math xmlns=""http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML""  alttext=""{\displaystyle \left\{S_{1},\ S_{2},\ S_{3},\dots \right\}}"">

</math></span><img src=""https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/89250263c344e97cc83f572e346064e5fbed19f8"" class=""mwe-math-fallback-image-inline"" aria-hidden=""true"" style=""vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:16.749ex; height:2.843ex;"" alt=""\left\{S_{1},\ S_{2},\ S_{3},\dots \right\}""/></span> tends to a <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Limit_of_a_sequence"" title=""Limit of a sequence"">limit</a>; that means that the partial sums become closer and closer to a given number when the number of their terms increases. More precisely, a series converges, if there exists a number <span class=""mwe-math-element""><span class=""mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y"" style=""display: none;""><math xmlns=""http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML""  alttext=""{\displaystyle \ell }"">

    <annotation encoding=""application/x-tex"">{\displaystyle \left|S_{n}-\ell \right\vert \leq \ \varepsilon .}</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math></span><img src=""https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/b8a11ae7f0b6f3f6bd68713334ff444ea123f8fb"" class=""mwe-math-fallback-image-inline"" aria-hidden=""true"" style=""vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:13.157ex; height:2.843ex;"" alt=""\left|S_{n}-\ell \right\vert \leq \ \varepsilon .""/></span></dd></dl>
If the series is convergent, the number <span class=""mwe-math-element""><span class=""mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y"" style=""display: none;""><math xmlns=""http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML""  alttext=""{\displaystyle \ell }"">
  <semantics>
    <mrow class=""MJX-TeXAtom-ORD"">
      <mstyle displaystyle=""true"" scriptlevel=""0"">
        <mi>&#x2113;<!-- ℓ --></mi>
      </mstyle>
    </mrow>
    <annotation encoding=""application/x-tex"">{\displaystyle \ell }</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math></span><img src=""https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/f066e981e530bacc07efc6a10fa82deee985929e"" class=""mwe-math-fallback-image-inline"" aria-hidden=""true"" style=""vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:0.97ex; height:2.176ex;"" alt=""\ell ""/></span> (necessarily unique) is called the <b>sum of the series</b>.

Any series that is not convergent is said to be <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Divergent_series"" title=""Divergent series"">divergent</a>.</dd>
<dt class=""glossary"" id=""convex_function"" style=""margin-top: 0.4em;""><dfn class=""glossary""><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Convex_function"" title=""Convex function"">Convex function</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class=""glossary"">In <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mathematics"" title=""Mathematics"">mathematics</a>, a <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Real-valued_function"" title=""Real-valued function"">real-valued function</a> defined on an <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Interval_(mathematics)#Multi-dimensional_intervals"" title=""Interval (mathematics)""><i>n</i>-dimensional interval</a> is called <b>convex</b> (or <b>convex downward</b> or <b>concave upward</b>) if the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Line_segment"" title=""Line segment"">line segment</a> between any two points on the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graph_of_a_function"" title=""Graph of a function"">graph of the function</a> lies above or on the graph, in a <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Euclidean_space"" title=""Euclidean space"">Euclidean space</a> (or more generally a <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vector_space"" title=""Vector space"">vector space</a>) of at least two dimensions. Equivalently, a function is convex if its <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Epigraph_(mathematics)"" title=""Epigraph (mathematics)"">epigraph</a> (the set of points on or above the graph of the function) is a <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Convex_set"" title=""Convex set"">convex set</a>. For a twice differentiable function of a single variable, if the second derivative is always greater than or equal to zero for its entire domain then the function is convex.<sup id=""cite_ref-21"" class=""reference""><a href=""#cite_note-21"">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> Well-known examples of convex functions include the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quadratic_function"" title=""Quadratic function"">quadratic function</a> <span class=""mwe-math-element""><span class=""mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y"" style=""display: none;""><math xmlns=""http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML""  alttext=""{\displaystyle x^{2}}"">
  <semantics>
    <mrow class=""MJX-TeXAtom-ORD"">
      <mstyle displaystyle=""true"" scriptlevel=""0"">
        <msup>
          <mi>x</mi>
          <mrow class=""MJX-TeXAtom-ORD"">
            <mn>2</mn>
          </mrow>
        </msup>
      </mstyle>
    </mrow>
    <annotation encoding=""application/x-tex"">{\displaystyle x^{2}}</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math></span><img src=""https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/cf0bf28fd28f45d07e1ceb909ce333c18c558c93"" class=""mwe-math-fallback-image-inline"" aria-hidden=""true"" style=""vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:2.384ex; height:2.676ex;"" alt=""x^{2}""/></span> and the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Exponential_function"" title=""Exponential function"">exponential function</a> <span class=""mwe-math-element""><span class=""mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y"" style=""display: none;""><math xmlns=""http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML""  alttext=""{\displaystyle e^{x}}"">

<dt class=""glossary"" id=""cramer&#39;s_rule"" style=""margin-top: 0.4em;""><dfn class=""glossary""><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cramer%27s_rule"" title=""Cramer&#39;s rule"">Cramer's rule</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class=""glossary""> In <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Linear_algebra"" title=""Linear algebra"">linear algebra</a>, <b>Cramer's rule</b> is an explicit formula for the solution of a <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/System_of_linear_equations"" title=""System of linear equations"">system of linear equations</a> with as many equations as unknowns, valid whenever the system has a unique solution. It expresses the solution in terms of the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Determinant"" title=""Determinant"">determinants</a> of the (square) coefficient <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Matrix_(mathematics)"" title=""Matrix (mathematics)"">matrix</a> and of matrices obtained from it by replacing one column by the column vector of right-hand-sides of the equations. It is named after <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gabriel_Cramer"" title=""Gabriel Cramer"">Gabriel Cramer</a> (1704&#8211;1752), who published the rule for an arbitrary number of unknowns in 1750,<sup id=""cite_ref-22"" class=""reference""><a href=""#cite_note-22"">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup><sup id=""cite_ref-23"" class=""reference""><a href=""#cite_note-23"">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> although <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Colin_Maclaurin"" title=""Colin Maclaurin"">Colin Maclaurin</a> also published special cases of the rule in 1748<sup id=""cite_ref-24"" class=""reference""><a href=""#cite_note-24"">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> (and possibly knew of it as early as 1729).<sup id=""cite_ref-25"" class=""reference""><a href=""#cite_note-25"">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup><sup id=""cite_ref-26"" class=""reference""><a href=""#cite_note-26"">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup><sup id=""cite_ref-27"" class=""reference""><a href=""#cite_note-27"">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup>.</dd>
<dt class=""glossary"" id=""critical_point"" style=""margin-top: 0.4em;""><dfn class=""glossary""><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Critical_point_(mathematics)"" title=""Critical point (mathematics)"">Critical point</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class=""glossary"">A <b>critical point</b> or <b>stationary point</b> of a <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Differentiable_function"" title=""Differentiable function"">differentiable function</a> of a <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Function_of_a_real_variable"" title=""Function of a real variable"">real</a> or <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Complex_variable"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Complex variable"">complex variable</a> is any value in its <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Domain_of_a_function"" title=""Domain of a function"">domain</a> where its <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Derivative"" title=""Derivative"">derivative</a> is 0.<sup id=""cite_ref-28"" class=""reference""><a href=""#cite_note-28"">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup><sup id=""cite_ref-29"" class=""reference""><a href=""#cite_note-29"">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup></dd>
<dt class=""glossary"" id=""curve"" style=""margin-top: 0.4em;""><dfn class=""glossary""><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Curve"" title=""Curve"">Curve</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class=""glossary"">A <b>curve</b> (also called a <b>curved line</b> in older texts) is, generally speaking, an object similar to a <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Line_(geometry)"" title=""Line (geometry)"">line</a> but that need not be <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Linearity"" title=""Linearity"">straight</a>.</dd>
<dt class=""glossary"" id=""curve_sketching"" style=""margin-top: 0.4em;""><dfn class=""glossary""><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Curve_sketching"" title=""Curve sketching"">Curve sketching</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class=""glossary"">In <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Geometry"" title=""Geometry"">geometry</a>, <b>curve sketching</b> (or <b>curve tracing</b>) includes techniques that can be used to produce a rough idea of overall shape of a <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Plane_curve"" title=""Plane curve"">plane curve</a> given its equation without computing the large numbers of points required for a detailed plot. It is an application of the theory of curves to find their main features. Here input is an equation.
 In <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Digital_geometry"" title=""Digital geometry"">digital geometry</a> it is a method of drawing a curve pixel by pixel. Here input is an array ( digital image).</dd>
<dt class=""glossary"" id=""damped_sine_wave"" style=""margin-top: 0.4em;""><dfn class=""glossary""><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Damped_sine_wave"" title=""Damped sine wave"">Damped sine wave</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class=""glossary"">Is a <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sine_wave"" title=""Sine wave"">sinusoidal function</a> whose amplitude approaches zero as time increases.<sup id=""cite_ref-30"" class=""reference""><a href=""#cite_note-30"">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup></dd>
<dt class=""glossary"" id=""degree_of_a_polynomial"" style=""margin-top: 0.4em;""><dfn class=""glossary""><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Degree_of_a_polynomial"" title=""Degree of a polynomial"">Degree of a polynomial</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class=""glossary"">Is the highest degree of its <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Monomial"" title=""Monomial"">monomials</a> (individual terms) with non-zero coefficients. The <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Degree_of_a_monomial"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Degree of a monomial"">degree of a term</a> is the sum of the exponents of the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Variable_(mathematics)"" title=""Variable (mathematics)"">variables</a> that appear in it, and thus is a non-negative integer.</dd>
<dt class=""glossary"" id=""derivative"" style=""margin-top: 0.4em;""><dfn class=""glossary""><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Derivative"" title=""Derivative"">Derivative</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class=""glossary"">The <b>derivative</b> of a <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Function_of_a_real_variable"" title=""Function of a real variable"">function of a real variable</a> measures the sensitivity to change of the function value (output value) with respect to a change in its argument (input value). Derivatives are a fundamental tool of <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Calculus"" title=""Calculus"">calculus</a>. For example, the derivative of the position of a moving object with respect to <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Time"" title=""Time"">time</a> is the object's <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Velocity"" title=""Velocity"">velocity</a>: this measures how quickly the position of the object changes when time advances.</dd>
<dt class=""glossary"" id=""derivative_test"" style=""margin-top: 0.4em;""><dfn class=""glossary""><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Derivative_test"" title=""Derivative test"">Derivative test</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class=""glossary"">A <b>derivative test</b> uses the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Derivative"" title=""Derivative"">derivatives</a> of a function to locate the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stationary_point"" title=""Stationary point"">critical points</a> of a function and determine whether each point is a <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Local_maximum"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Local maximum"">local maximum</a>, a <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Local_minimum"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Local minimum"">local minimum</a>, or a <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Saddle_point"" title=""Saddle point"">saddle point</a>. Derivative tests can also give information about the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Concave_function"" title=""Concave function"">concavity</a> of a function.</dd>
<dt class=""glossary"" id=""differentiable_function"" style=""margin-top: 0.4em;""><dfn class=""glossary""><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Differentiable_function"" title=""Differentiable function"">Differentiable function</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class=""glossary"">A <b>differentiable function</b> of one <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Real_number"" title=""Real number"">real</a> variable is a function whose <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Derivative"" title=""Derivative"">derivative</a> exists at each point in its <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Domain_of_a_function"" title=""Domain of a function"">domain</a>. As a result, the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graph_of_a_function"" title=""Graph of a function"">graph</a> of a differentiable function must have a (non-<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vertical_tangent"" title=""Vertical tangent"">vertical</a>) <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tangent_line"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Tangent line"">tangent line</a> at each point in its domain, be relatively smooth, and cannot contain any breaks, bends, or <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cusp_(singularity)"" title=""Cusp (singularity)"">cusps</a>.</dd>
<dt class=""glossary"" id=""differential_(infinitesimal)"" style=""margin-top: 0.4em;""><dfn class=""glossary""><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Differential_(infinitesimal)"" title=""Differential (infinitesimal)"">Differential (infinitesimal)</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class=""glossary"">The term <b>differential</b> is used in <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Calculus"" title=""Calculus"">calculus</a> to refer to an <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Infinitesimal"" title=""Infinitesimal"">infinitesimal</a> (infinitely small) change in some <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Variable_(mathematics)"" title=""Variable (mathematics)"">varying quantity</a>. For example, if <i>x</i> is a <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Variable_(mathematics)"" title=""Variable (mathematics)"">variable</a>, then a change in the value of <i>x</i> is often denoted Δ<i>x</i> (pronounced <i><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Delta_(Greek)"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Delta (Greek)"">delta</a> x</i>). The differential <i>dx</i> represents an infinitely small change in the variable <i>x</i>. The idea of an infinitely small or infinitely slow change is extremely useful intuitively, and there are a number of ways to make the notion mathematically precise.

Using calculus, it is possible to relate the infinitely small changes of various variables to each other mathematically using <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Derivative"" title=""Derivative"">derivatives</a>. If <i>y</i> is a function of <i>x</i>, then the differential <i>dy</i> of <i>y</i> is related to <i>dx</i> by the formula
<dl><dd><span class=""mwe-math-element""><span class=""mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y"" style=""display: none;""><math xmlns=""http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML""  alttext=""{\displaystyle dy={\frac {dy}{dx}}\,dx,}"">
  <semantics>
    <mrow class=""MJX-TeXAtom-ORD"">
      <mstyle displaystyle=""true"" scriptlevel=""0"">
        <mi>d</mi>
        <mi>y</mi>
        <mo>=</mo>
        <mrow class=""MJX-TeXAtom-ORD"">

            </mrow>
            <mrow>
              <mi>d</mi>

            </mrow>
          </mfrac>
        </mrow>
        <mspace width=""thinmathspace"" />
        <mi>d</mi>
        <mi>x</mi>
        <mo>,</mo>
      </mstyle>
    </mrow>
    <annotation encoding=""application/x-tex"">{\displaystyle dy={\frac {dy}{dx}}\,dx,}</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math></span><img src=""https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/e8690f9df1066aadc3c2999e8633a040038558df"" class=""mwe-math-fallback-image-inline"" aria-hidden=""true"" style=""vertical-align: -2.005ex; width:12.431ex; height:5.509ex;"" alt=""{\displaystyle dy={\frac {dy}{dx}}\,dx,}""/></span></dd></dl>
where <i>dy</i>/<i>dx</i> denotes the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Derivative"" title=""Derivative"">derivative</a> of <i>y</i> with respect to <i>x</i>. This formula summarizes the intuitive idea that the derivative of <i>y</i> with respect to <i>x</i> is the limit of the ratio of differences Δ<i>y</i>/Δ<i>x</i> as Δ<i>x</i> becomes infinitesimal.</dd>
<dt class=""glossary"" id=""differential_calculus"" style=""margin-top: 0.4em;""><dfn class=""glossary""><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Differential_calculus"" title=""Differential calculus"">Differential calculus</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class=""glossary"">Is a subfield of calculus<sup id=""cite_ref-31"" class=""reference""><a href=""#cite_note-31"">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> concerned with the study of the rates at which quantities change. It is one of the two traditional divisions of calculus, the other being <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Integral_calculus"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Integral calculus"">integral calculus</a>, the study of the area beneath a curve.<sup id=""cite_ref-32"" class=""reference""><a href=""#cite_note-32"">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup></dd>
<dt class=""glossary"" id=""differential_equation"" style=""margin-top: 0.4em;""><dfn class=""glossary""><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Differential_equation"" title=""Differential equation"">Differential equation</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class=""glossary"">Is a <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mathematics"" title=""Mathematics"">mathematical</a> <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Equation"" title=""Equation"">equation</a> that relates some <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Function_(mathematics)"" title=""Function (mathematics)"">function</a> with its <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Derivative"" title=""Derivative"">derivatives</a>. In applications, the functions usually represent physical quantities, the derivatives represent their rates of change, and the equation defines a relationship between the two.</dd>
<dt class=""glossary"" id=""differential_operator"" style=""margin-top: 0.4em;""><dfn class=""glossary""><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Differential_operator"" title=""Differential operator"">Differential operator</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class=""glossary"">.</dd>
<dt class=""glossary"" id=""differential_of_a_function"" style=""margin-top: 0.4em;""><dfn class=""glossary""><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Differential_of_a_function"" title=""Differential of a function"">Differential of a function</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class=""glossary"">In <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Calculus"" title=""Calculus"">calculus</a>, the <b>differential</b> represents the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Principal_part#Calculus"" title=""Principal part"">principal part</a> of the change in a function <i>y</i>&#160;=&#160;<i>f</i>(<i>x</i>) with respect to changes in the independent variable. The differential <i>dy</i> is defined by
<dl><dd><span class=""mwe-math-element""><span class=""mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y"" style=""display: none;""><math xmlns=""http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML""  alttext=""{\displaystyle dy=f'(x)\,dx,}"">

where <span class=""mwe-math-element""><span class=""mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y"" style=""display: none;""><math xmlns=""http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML""  alttext=""{\displaystyle f'(x)}"">

</math></span><img src=""https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/d0cd7d7c75340e779d82658e19d1720ce84ab127"" class=""mwe-math-fallback-image-inline"" aria-hidden=""true"" style=""vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:5.144ex; height:3.009ex;"" alt=""f&#039;(x)""/></span> is the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Derivative"" title=""Derivative"">derivative</a> of <i>f</i> with respect to <i>x</i>, and <i>dx</i> is an additional real <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Variable_(mathematics)"" title=""Variable (mathematics)"">variable</a> (so that <i>dy</i> is a function of <i>x</i> and <i>dx</i>). The notation is such that the equation

<dl><dd><span class=""mwe-math-element""><span class=""mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y"" style=""display: none;""><math xmlns=""http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML""  alttext=""{\displaystyle dy={\frac {dy}{dx}}\,dx}"">

</math></span><img src=""https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/c4af882e478e7345731264e1984ccdbc067af8df"" class=""mwe-math-fallback-image-inline"" aria-hidden=""true"" style=""vertical-align: -2.005ex; width:11.784ex; height:5.509ex;"" alt=""dy={\frac  {dy}{dx}}\,dx""/></span></dd></dl>

holds, where the derivative is represented in the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Leibniz_notation"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Leibniz notation"">Leibniz notation</a> <i>dy</i>/<i>dx</i>, and this is consistent with regarding the derivative as the quotient of the differentials. One also writes

<dl><dd><span class=""mwe-math-element""><span class=""mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y"" style=""display: none;""><math xmlns=""http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML""  alttext=""{\displaystyle df(x)=f'(x)\,dx.}"">
  <semantics>
    <mrow class=""MJX-TeXAtom-ORD"">
      <mstyle displaystyle=""true"" scriptlevel=""0"">

        <mo>.</mo>

    <annotation encoding=""application/x-tex"">{\displaystyle df(x)=f'(x)\,dx.}</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math></span><img src=""https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/1036d9e3de8d36ab7126d94f2e0603458b3307b4"" class=""mwe-math-fallback-image-inline"" aria-hidden=""true"" style=""vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:17.456ex; height:3.009ex;"" alt=""df(x)=f&#039;(x)\,dx.""/></span></dd></dl>

The precise meaning of the variables <i>dy</i> and <i>dx</i> depends on the context of the application and the required level of mathematical rigor. The domain of these variables may take on a particular geometrical significance if the differential is regarded as a particular <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Differential_form"" title=""Differential form"">differential form</a>, or analytical significance if the differential is regarded as a <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Linear_approximation"" title=""Linear approximation"">linear approximation</a> to the increment of a function. Traditionally, the variables <i>dx</i> and <i>dy</i> are considered to be very small (<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Infinitesimal"" title=""Infinitesimal"">infinitesimal</a>), and this interpretation is made rigorous in <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Non-standard_analysis"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Non-standard analysis"">non-standard analysis</a>.</dd>
<dt class=""glossary"" id=""differentiation_rules"" style=""margin-top: 0.4em;""><dfn class=""glossary""><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Differentiation_rules"" title=""Differentiation rules"">Differentiation rules</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class=""glossary"">.</dd>
<dt class=""glossary"" id=""direct_comparison_test"" style=""margin-top: 0.4em;""><dfn class=""glossary""><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Direct_comparison_test"" title=""Direct comparison test"">Direct comparison test</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class=""glossary"">A convergence test in which an infinite series or an improper integral is compared to one with known convergence properties.</dd>
<dt class=""glossary"" id=""dirichlet&#39;s_test"" style=""margin-top: 0.4em;""><dfn class=""glossary""><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dirichlet%27s_test"" title=""Dirichlet&#39;s test"">Dirichlet's test</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class=""glossary"">Is a method of testing for the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Convergent_series"" title=""Convergent series"">convergence</a> of a <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Series_(mathematics)"" title=""Series (mathematics)"">series</a>. It is named after its author <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Peter_Gustav_Lejeune_Dirichlet"" title=""Peter Gustav Lejeune Dirichlet"">Peter Gustav Lejeune Dirichlet</a>, and was published posthumously in the <i><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Journal_de_Math%C3%A9matiques_Pures_et_Appliqu%C3%A9es"" title=""Journal de Mathématiques Pures et Appliquées"">Journal de Mathématiques Pures et Appliquées</a></i> in 1862.<sup id=""cite_ref-33"" class=""reference""><a href=""#cite_note-33"">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> The test states that if <span class=""mwe-math-element""><span class=""mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y"" style=""display: none;""><math xmlns=""http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML""  alttext=""{\displaystyle \{a_{n}\}}"">

<dt class=""glossary"" id=""disc_integration"" style=""margin-top: 0.4em;""><dfn class=""glossary""><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Disc_integration"" title=""Disc integration"">Disc integration</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class=""glossary"">Also known in <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Integral_calculus"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Integral calculus"">integral calculus</a> as the <b>disc method</b>, is a means of calculating the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Volume"" title=""Volume"">volume</a> of a <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Solid_of_revolution"" title=""Solid of revolution"">solid of revolution</a> of a solid-state material when <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Integral"" title=""Integral"">integrating</a> along an axis ""parallel"" to the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Axis_of_revolution"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Axis of revolution"">axis of revolution</a>.</dd>
<dt class=""glossary"" id=""divergent_series"" style=""margin-top: 0.4em;""><dfn class=""glossary""><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Divergent_series"" title=""Divergent series"">Divergent series</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class=""glossary"">Is an <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Infinite_series"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Infinite series"">infinite series</a> that is not <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Convergent_series"" title=""Convergent series"">convergent</a>, meaning that the infinite <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sequence"" title=""Sequence"">sequence</a> of the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Partial_sum"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Partial sum"">partial sums</a> of the series does not have a finite <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Limit_of_a_sequence"" title=""Limit of a sequence"">limit</a>.</dd>
<dt class=""glossary"" id=""discontinuity"" style=""margin-top: 0.4em;""><dfn class=""glossary""><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Discontinuity_(mathematics)"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Discontinuity (mathematics)"">Discontinuity</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class=""glossary""><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Continuous_function"" title=""Continuous function"">Continuous functions</a> are of utmost importance in <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mathematics"" title=""Mathematics"">mathematics</a>, functions and applications. However, not all <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Function_(mathematics)"" title=""Function (mathematics)"">functions</a> are continuous. If a function is not continuous at a point in its <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Domain_(mathematics)"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Domain (mathematics)"">domain</a>, one says that it has a <b>discontinuity</b> there. The set of all points of discontinuity of a function may be a <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Discrete_set"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Discrete set"">discrete set</a>, a <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dense_set"" title=""Dense set"">dense set</a>, or even the entire domain of the function.</dd>
<dt class=""glossary"" id=""dot_product"" style=""margin-top: 0.4em;""><dfn class=""glossary""><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dot_product"" title=""Dot product"">Dot product</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class=""glossary"">In <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mathematics"" title=""Mathematics"">mathematics</a>, the <b>dot product</b> or <b>scalar product</b><sup id=""cite_ref-34"" class=""reference""><a href=""#cite_note-34"">&#91;note 1&#93;</a></sup> is an <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Algebraic_operation"" title=""Algebraic operation"">algebraic operation</a> that takes two equal-length sequences of numbers (usually <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Coordinate_vector"" title=""Coordinate vector"">coordinate vectors</a>) and returns a single number. In <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Euclidean_geometry"" title=""Euclidean geometry"">Euclidean geometry</a>, the dot product of the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cartesian_coordinates"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Cartesian coordinates"">Cartesian coordinates</a> of two <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vector_(mathematics_and_physics)"" title=""Vector (mathematics and physics)"">vectors</a> is widely used and often called ""the"" <b>inner product</b> (or rarely <b>projection product</b>) of Euclidean space even though it is not the only inner product that can be defined on Euclidean space; see also <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Inner_product_space"" title=""Inner product space"">inner product space</a>.</dd>
<dt class=""glossary"" id=""double_integral"" style=""margin-top: 0.4em;""><dfn class=""glossary""><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Double_integral"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Double integral"">Double integral</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class=""glossary"">The <b>multiple integral</b> is a <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Definite_integral"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Definite integral"">definite integral</a> of a <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Function_(mathematics)"" title=""Function (mathematics)"">function</a> of more than one real <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Variable_(mathematics)"" title=""Variable (mathematics)"">variable</a>, for example, <span class=""texhtml""><i>f</i>(<i>x</i>, <i>y</i>)</span> or <span class=""texhtml""><i>f</i>(<i>x</i>, <i>y</i>, <i>z</i>)</span>. Integrals of a function of two variables over a region in <span class=""texhtml""><b>R</b><sup>2</sup></span> are called <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Double_integrals"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Double integrals"">double integrals</a>, and integrals of a function of three variables over a region of <span class=""texhtml""><b>R</b><sup>3</sup></span> are called <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Triple_integrals"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Triple integrals"">triple integrals</a>.<sup id=""cite_ref-Stewart_35-0"" class=""reference""><a href=""#cite_note-Stewart-35"">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup></dd>
<dt class=""glossary"" id=""e_(mathematical_constant)"" style=""margin-top: 0.4em;""><dfn class=""glossary""><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/E_(mathematical_constant)"" title=""E (mathematical constant)"">e (mathematical constant)</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class=""glossary"">The number <b><span class=""texhtml mvar"" style=""font-style:italic;"">e</span></b> is a <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mathematical_constant"" title=""Mathematical constant"">mathematical constant</a> that is the base of the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Natural_logarithm"" title=""Natural logarithm"">natural logarithm</a>: the unique number whose natural logarithm is equal to one. It is approximately equal to <b>2.71828</b>,<sup id=""cite_ref-36"" class=""reference""><a href=""#cite_note-36"">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> and is the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Limit_of_a_sequence"" title=""Limit of a sequence"">limit</a> of <span class=""texhtml"">(1 + 1/<i>n</i>)<sup><i>n</i></sup></span> as <span class=""texhtml mvar"" style=""font-style:italic;"">n</span> approaches <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Infinity"" title=""Infinity"">infinity</a>, an expression that arises in the study of <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Compound_interest"" title=""Compound interest"">compound interest</a>. It can also be calculated as the sum of the infinite <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Series_(mathematics)"" title=""Series (mathematics)"">series</a><sup id=""cite_ref-37"" class=""reference""><a href=""#cite_note-37"">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup>

  </semantics>
</math></span><img src=""https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/534a55992d112b62c1163de47b1dd9268f20153a"" class=""mwe-math-fallback-image-inline"" aria-hidden=""true"" style=""vertical-align: -3.005ex; width:44.504ex; height:6.843ex;"" alt=""{\displaystyle e=\displaystyle \sum \limits _{n=0}^{\infty }{\dfrac {1}{n!}}={\frac {1}{1}}+{\frac {1}{1}}+{\frac {1}{1\cdot 2}}+{\frac {1}{1\cdot 2\cdot 3}}+\cdots }""/></span></dd></dl></dd></dl>
<dt class=""glossary"" id=""elliptic_integral"" style=""margin-top: 0.4em;""><dfn class=""glossary""><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Elliptic_integral"" title=""Elliptic integral"">Elliptic integral</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class=""glossary"">In <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Integral_calculus"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Integral calculus"">integral calculus</a>, <b>elliptic integrals</b> originally arose in connection with the problem of giving the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arc_length"" title=""Arc length"">arc length</a> of an <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ellipse"" title=""Ellipse"">ellipse</a>. They were first studied by <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Giulio_Carlo_de%27_Toschi_di_Fagnano"" title=""Giulio Carlo de&#39; Toschi di Fagnano"">Giulio Fagnano</a> and <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Leonhard_Euler"" title=""Leonhard Euler"">Leonhard Euler</a> (<abbr title=""circa"">c.</abbr><span style=""white-space:nowrap;"">&#8201;1750</span>). Modern mathematics defines an ""elliptic integral"" as any <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Function_(mathematics)"" title=""Function (mathematics)"">function</a> <span class=""texhtml""><i>f</i></span> which can be expressed in the form

<dl><dd><span class=""mwe-math-element""><span class=""mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y"" style=""display: none;""><math xmlns=""http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML""  alttext=""{\displaystyle f(x)=\int _{c}^{x}R\left(t,{\sqrt {P(t)}}\right)\,dt,}"">
  <semantics>
    <mrow class=""MJX-TeXAtom-ORD"">
      <mstyle displaystyle=""true"" scriptlevel=""0"">

          <mrow class=""MJX-TeXAtom-ORD"">
            <mi>x</mi>
          </mrow>
        </msubsup>
        <mi>R</mi>
        <mrow>
          <mo>(</mo>
          <mrow>
            <mi>t</mi>
            <mo>,</mo>
            <mrow class=""MJX-TeXAtom-ORD"">
              <msqrt>

                <mo stretchy=""false"">(</mo>

        <mspace width=""thinmathspace"" />

      </mstyle>
    </mrow>
    <annotation encoding=""application/x-tex"">{\displaystyle f(x)=\int _{c}^{x}R\left(t,{\sqrt {P(t)}}\right)\,dt,}</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math></span><img src=""https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/066d7c985940c1aa0b4458a9ce2abafe1c3c6fcd"" class=""mwe-math-fallback-image-inline"" aria-hidden=""true"" style=""vertical-align: -2.338ex; width:28.535ex; height:5.843ex;"" alt="" f(x) = \int_{c}^{x} R \left(t, \sqrt{P(t)} \right) \, dt, ""/></span></dd></dl>

where <span class=""texhtml""><i>R</i></span> is a <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rational_function"" title=""Rational function"">rational function</a> of its two arguments, <span class=""texhtml""><i>P</i></span> is a <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polynomial"" title=""Polynomial"">polynomial</a> of degree 3 or 4 with no repeated roots, and <span class=""texhtml""><i>c</i></span> is a constant..</dd>
<dt class=""glossary"" id=""essential_discontinuity"" style=""margin-top: 0.4em;""><dfn class=""glossary""><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Classification_of_discontinuities#Essential_discontinuity"" title=""Classification of discontinuities"">Essential discontinuity</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class=""glossary"">For an essential discontinuity, only one of the two one-sided limits needs not exist or be infinite.

In this case, <span class=""mwe-math-element""><span class=""mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y"" style=""display: none;""><math xmlns=""http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML""  alttext=""{\displaystyle \scriptstyle L^{-}}"">

So <i>x</i><sub>0</sub> is an <i>essential discontinuity</i>, <i>infinite discontinuity</i>, or <i>discontinuity of the second kind</i>. (This is distinct from the term <i><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Essential_singularity"" title=""Essential singularity"">essential singularity</a></i> which is often used when studying <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Complex_analysis"" title=""Complex analysis"">functions of complex variables</a>.</dd>
<dt class=""glossary"" id=""euler_method"" style=""margin-top: 0.4em;""><dfn class=""glossary""><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Euler_method"" title=""Euler method"">Euler method</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class=""glossary"">Euler's method is a numerical method to solve first order first degree differential equation with a given initial value. It is the most basic <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Explicit_and_implicit_methods"" title=""Explicit and implicit methods"">explicit method</a> for <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Numerical_ordinary_differential_equations"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Numerical ordinary differential equations"">numerical integration of ordinary differential equations</a> and is the simplest <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Runge%E2%80%93Kutta_method"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Runge–Kutta method"">Runge–Kutta method</a>. The Euler method is named after <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Leonhard_Euler"" title=""Leonhard Euler"">Leonhard Euler</a>, who treated it in his book <i><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Institutionum_calculi_integralis"" title=""Institutionum calculi integralis"">Institutionum calculi integralis</a></i> (published 1768–1870).<sup id=""cite_ref-38"" class=""reference""><a href=""#cite_note-38"">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup></dd>
<dt class=""glossary"" id=""exponential_function"" style=""margin-top: 0.4em;""><dfn class=""glossary""><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Exponential_function"" title=""Exponential function"">Exponential function</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class=""glossary"">In <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mathematics"" title=""Mathematics"">mathematics</a>, an <b>exponential function</b> is a function of the form
<div style=""padding-left: 3em; padding-right:0em; overflow: hidden;""><span class=""mwe-math-element""><span class=""mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y"" style=""display: none;""><math xmlns=""http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML""  alttext=""{\displaystyle f(x)=ab^{x},}"">
  <semantics>
    <mrow class=""MJX-TeXAtom-ORD"">
      <mstyle displaystyle=""true"" scriptlevel=""0"">
        <mi>f</mi>
        <mo stretchy=""false"">(</mo>
        <mi>x</mi>
        <mo stretchy=""false"">)</mo>
        <mo>=</mo>
        <mi>a</mi>
        <msup>
          <mi>b</mi>
          <mrow class=""MJX-TeXAtom-ORD"">
            <mi>x</mi>
          </mrow>
        </msup>
        <mo>,</mo>
      </mstyle>
    </mrow>
    <annotation encoding=""application/x-tex"">{\displaystyle f(x)=ab^{x},}</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math></span><img src=""https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/b8f7e0207f9410fd16506b8cab68b31b78c4d219"" class=""mwe-math-fallback-image-inline"" aria-hidden=""true"" style=""vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:11.563ex; height:2.843ex;"" alt=""{\displaystyle f(x)=ab^{x},}""/></span></div>
<p>where <span class=""texhtml mvar"" style=""font-style:italic;"">b</span> is a positive real number, and in which the argument <span class=""texhtml""><i>x</i></span> occurs as an exponent. For real numbers <span class=""texhtml mvar"" style=""font-style:italic;"">c</span> and <span class=""texhtml mvar"" style=""font-style:italic;"">d,</span> a function of the form <span class=""mwe-math-element""><span class=""mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y"" style=""display: none;""><math xmlns=""http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML""  alttext=""{\displaystyle f(x)=ab^{cx+d}}"">
  <semantics>
    <mrow class=""MJX-TeXAtom-ORD"">
      <mstyle displaystyle=""true"" scriptlevel=""0"">
        <mi>f</mi>
        <mo stretchy=""false"">(</mo>

        <mo stretchy=""false"">)</mo>

        <mi>a</mi>
        <msup>
          <mi>b</mi>

            <mi>x</mi>
            <mo>+</mo>
            <mi>d</mi>
          </mrow>
        </msup>
      </mstyle>
    </mrow>

  </semantics>
