<dt class=""glossary"" id=""extreme_value_theorem"" style=""margin-top: 0.4em;""><dfn class=""glossary""><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Extreme_value_theorem"" title=""Extreme value theorem"">Extreme value theorem</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class=""glossary"">States that if a real-valued <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Function_(mathematics)"" title=""Function (mathematics)"">function</a> <i>f</i> is <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Continuous_function"" title=""Continuous function"">continuous</a> on the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bounded_interval#Classification_of_intervals"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Bounded interval"">closed</a> interval [<i>a</i>,<i>b</i>], then <i>f</i> must attain a <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Maximum"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Maximum"">maximum</a> and a <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Minimum"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Minimum"">minimum</a>, each at least once. That is, there exist numbers <i>c</i> and <i>d</i> in [<i>a</i>,<i>b</i>] such that:

<dl><dd><span class=""mwe-math-element""><span class=""mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y"" style=""display: none;""><math xmlns=""http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML""  alttext=""{\displaystyle f(c)\geq f(x)\geq f(d)\quad {\text{for all }}x\in [a,b].}"">
  <semantics>
    <mrow class=""MJX-TeXAtom-ORD"">
      <mstyle displaystyle=""true"" scriptlevel=""0"">
        <mi>f</mi>
        <mo stretchy=""false"">(</mo>
        <mi>c</mi>
        <mo stretchy=""false"">)</mo>
        <mo>&#x2265;<!-- ≥ --></mo>
        <mi>f</mi>
        <mo stretchy=""false"">(</mo>
        <mi>x</mi>
        <mo stretchy=""false"">)</mo>
        <mo>&#x2265;<!-- ≥ --></mo>
        <mi>f</mi>
        <mo stretchy=""false"">(</mo>
        <mi>d</mi>
        <mo stretchy=""false"">)</mo>
        <mspace width=""1em"" />
        <mrow class=""MJX-TeXAtom-ORD"">
          <mtext>for all&#xA0;</mtext>
        </mrow>
        <mi>x</mi>
        <mo>&#x2208;<!-- ∈ --></mo>
        <mo stretchy=""false"">[</mo>
        <mi>a</mi>
        <mo>,</mo>
        <mi>b</mi>
        <mo stretchy=""false"">]</mo>
        <mo>.</mo>
      </mstyle>
    </mrow>
    <annotation encoding=""application/x-tex"">{\displaystyle f(c)\geq f(x)\geq f(d)\quad {\text{for all }}x\in [a,b].}</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math></span><img src=""https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/8ccc399772dc99e3a3970432b84382c0ffda9560"" class=""mwe-math-fallback-image-inline"" aria-hidden=""true"" style=""vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:37.111ex; height:2.843ex;"" alt=""{\displaystyle f(c)\geq f(x)\geq f(d)\quad {\text{for all }}x\in [a,b].}""/></span></dd></dl>

A related theorem is <b>the boundedness theorem</b> which states that a continuous function <i>f</i> in the closed interval [<i>a</i>,<i>b</i>] is <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bounded_function"" title=""Bounded function"">bounded</a> on that interval. That is, there exist real numbers <i>m</i> and <i>M</i> such that:

<dl><dd><span class=""mwe-math-element""><span class=""mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y"" style=""display: none;""><math xmlns=""http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML""  alttext=""{\displaystyle m&lt;f(x)&lt;M\quad {\text{for all }}x\in [a,b].}"">
  <semantics>
    <mrow class=""MJX-TeXAtom-ORD"">
      <mstyle displaystyle=""true"" scriptlevel=""0"">
        <mi>m</mi>
        <mo>&lt;</mo>
        <mi>f</mi>
        <mo stretchy=""false"">(</mo>
        <mi>x</mi>
        <mo stretchy=""false"">)</mo>
        <mo>&lt;</mo>
        <mi>M</mi>
        <mspace width=""1em"" />
        <mrow class=""MJX-TeXAtom-ORD"">
          <mtext>for all&#xA0;</mtext>
        </mrow>
        <mi>x</mi>
        <mo>&#x2208;<!-- ∈ --></mo>
        <mo stretchy=""false"">[</mo>
        <mi>a</mi>
        <mo>,</mo>
        <mi>b</mi>
        <mo stretchy=""false"">]</mo>
        <mo>.</mo>
      </mstyle>
    </mrow>
    <annotation encoding=""application/x-tex"">{\displaystyle m&lt;f(x)&lt;M\quad {\text{for all }}x\in [a,b].}</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math></span><img src=""https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/bea84dcd500745a81c211a93cc3b650db8ea98d5"" class=""mwe-math-fallback-image-inline"" aria-hidden=""true"" style=""vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:33.195ex; height:2.843ex;"" alt=""{\displaystyle m&lt;f(x)&lt;M\quad {\text{for all }}x\in [a,b].}""/></span></dd></dl>

The extreme value theorem enriches the boundedness theorem by saying that not only is the function bounded, but it also attains its least upper bound as its maximum and its greatest lower bound as its minimum.</dd>
<dt class=""glossary"" id=""extremum"" style=""margin-top: 0.4em;""><dfn class=""glossary""><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Maxima_and_minima"" title=""Maxima and minima"">Extremum</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class=""glossary"">In <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mathematical_analysis"" title=""Mathematical analysis"">mathematical analysis</a>, the <b>maxima and minima</b> (the respective plurals of <b>maximum</b> and <b>minimum</b>) of a <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Function_(mathematics)"" title=""Function (mathematics)"">function</a>, known collectively as <b>extrema</b> (the plural of <b>extremum</b>), are the largest and smallest value of the function, either within a given range (the <b>local</b> or <b>relative</b> extrema) or on the entire <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Domain_of_a_function"" title=""Domain of a function"">domain of a function</a> (the <b>global</b> or <b>absolute</b> extrema).<sup id=""cite_ref-39"" class=""reference""><a href=""#cite_note-39"">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup><sup id=""cite_ref-40"" class=""reference""><a href=""#cite_note-40"">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup><sup id=""cite_ref-41"" class=""reference""><a href=""#cite_note-41"">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pierre_de_Fermat"" title=""Pierre de Fermat"">Pierre de Fermat</a> was one of the first mathematicians to propose a general technique, <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Adequality"" title=""Adequality"">adequality</a>, for finding the maxima and minima of functions.

As defined in <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Set_theory"" title=""Set theory"">set theory</a>, the maximum and minimum of a <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Set_(mathematics)"" title=""Set (mathematics)"">set</a> are the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Greatest_and_least_elements"" title=""Greatest and least elements"">greatest and least elements</a> in the set, respectively. Unbounded infinite sets, such as the set of <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Real_number"" title=""Real number"">real numbers</a>, have no minimum or maximum.</dd>
<dt class=""glossary"" id=""faà_di_bruno&#39;s_formula"" style=""margin-top: 0.4em;""><dfn class=""glossary""><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fa%C3%A0_di_Bruno%27s_formula"" title=""Faà di Bruno&#39;s formula"">Faà di Bruno's formula</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class=""glossary"">Is an identity in <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mathematics"" title=""Mathematics"">mathematics</a> generalizing the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chain_rule"" title=""Chain rule"">chain rule</a> to higher derivatives, named after <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Francesco_Fa%C3%A0_di_Bruno"" title=""Francesco Faà di Bruno"">Francesco&#32;Faà di Bruno</a>&#160;(<a href=""#CITEREFFaà_di_Bruno1855"">1855</a>, <a href=""#CITEREFFaà_di_Bruno1857"">1857</a>), though he was not the first to state or prove the formula. In 1800, more than 50 years before Faà di Bruno, the French mathematician <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Louis_Fran%C3%A7ois_Antoine_Arbogast"" title=""Louis François Antoine Arbogast"">Louis François Antoine Arbogast</a> stated the formula in a calculus textbook,<sup id=""cite_ref-42"" class=""reference""><a href=""#cite_note-42"">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> considered the first published reference on the subject.<sup id=""cite_ref-43"" class=""reference""><a href=""#cite_note-43"">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup>

Perhaps the most well-known form of Faà di Bruno's formula says that

Combining the terms with the same value of <i>m</i><sub>1</sub>&#160;+&#160;<i>m</i><sub>2</sub>&#160;+&#160;...&#160;+&#160;<i>m</i><sub><i>n</i></sub>&#160;=&#160;<i>k</i> and noticing that <i>m</i><sub>&#160;<i>j</i></sub> has to be zero for <i>j</i>&#160;&gt;&#160;<i>n</i>&#160;&#8722;&#160;<i>k</i>&#160;+&#160;1 leads to a somewhat simpler formula expressed in terms of <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bell_polynomial"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Bell polynomial"">Bell polynomials</a> <i>B</i><sub><i>n</i>,<i>k</i></sub>(<i>x</i><sub>1</sub>,...,<i>x</i><sub><i>n</i>&#8722;<i>k</i>+1</sub>):

<dl><dd><span class=""mwe-math-element""><span class=""mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y"" style=""display: none;""><math xmlns=""http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML""  alttext=""{\displaystyle {d^{n} \over dx^{n}}f(g(x))=\sum _{k=1}^{n}f^{(k)}(g(x))\cdot B_{n,k}\left(g'(x),g''(x),\dots ,g^{(n-k+1)}(x)\right).}"">

            <mo>,</mo>
            <msup>
              <mi>g</mi>

    <annotation encoding=""application/x-tex"">{\displaystyle {d^{n} \over dx^{n}}f(g(x))=\sum _{k=1}^{n}f^{(k)}(g(x))\cdot B_{n,k}\left(g'(x),g''(x),\dots ,g^{(n-k+1)}(x)\right).}</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math></span><img src=""https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/c5e9078bc352969d6e2aed7629d56dbbdfca0209"" class=""mwe-math-fallback-image-inline"" aria-hidden=""true"" style=""vertical-align: -3.005ex; width:66.247ex; height:6.843ex;"" alt=""{d^n \over dx^n} f(g(x)) = \sum_{k=1}^n f^{(k)}(g(x))\cdot B_{n,k}\left(g&#039;(x),g&#039;&#039;(x),\dots,g^{(n-k+1)}(x)\right).""/></span></dd></dl></dd></dl>
<dt class=""glossary"" id=""first-degree_polynomial"" style=""margin-top: 0.4em;""><dfn class=""glossary""><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Degree_of_a_polynomial"" title=""Degree of a polynomial"">First-degree polynomial</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class=""glossary""></dd>
<dt class=""glossary"" id=""first_derivative_test"" style=""margin-top: 0.4em;""><dfn class=""glossary""><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Derivative_test#First_derivative_test"" title=""Derivative test"">First derivative test</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class=""glossary"">The first derivative test examines a function's <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Monotonic_function"" title=""Monotonic function"">monotonic</a> properties (where the function is increasing or decreasing) focusing on a particular point in its domain. If the function ""switches"" from increasing to decreasing at the point, then the function will achieve a highest value at that point. Similarly, if the function ""switches"" from decreasing to increasing at the point, then it will achieve a least value at that point. If the function fails to ""switch"", and remains increasing or remains decreasing, then no highest or least value is achieved.</dd>
<dt class=""glossary"" id=""fractional_calculus"" style=""margin-top: 0.4em;""><dfn class=""glossary""><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fractional_calculus"" title=""Fractional calculus"">Fractional calculus</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class=""glossary"">Is a branch of <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mathematical_analysis"" title=""Mathematical analysis"">mathematical analysis</a> that studies the several different possibilities of defining <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Real_number"" title=""Real number"">real number</a> powers or <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Complex_number"" title=""Complex number"">complex number</a> powers of the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Derivative"" title=""Derivative"">differentiation operator</a> <span class=""texhtml mvar"" style=""font-style:italic;"">D</span>

<dl><dd><span class=""mwe-math-element""><span class=""mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y"" style=""display: none;""><math xmlns=""http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML""  alttext=""{\displaystyle Df(x)={\dfrac {d}{dx}}f(x)}"">

and of the integration operator <span class=""texhtml mvar"" style=""font-style:italic;"">J</span>

In this context, the term <i>powers</i> refers to iterative application of a linear operator to a function, in some analogy to <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Function_composition"" title=""Function composition"">function composition</a> acting on a variable, i.e. <span class=""texhtml""><i>f</i>&#160;<sup><small>∘2</small></sup>(<i>x</i>)&#160;=&#160;<i>f</i>&#160;∘&#160;<i>f</i>&#160;(<i>x</i>)&#160;=&#160;<i>f</i>&#160;(&#160;<i>f</i>&#160;(<i>x</i>)&#160;)</span>.</dd>
<dt class=""glossary"" id=""frustum"" style=""margin-top: 0.4em;""><dfn class=""glossary""><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Frustum"" title=""Frustum"">Frustum</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class=""glossary"">In <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Geometry"" title=""Geometry"">geometry</a>, a <b>frustum</b> (plural: <i>frusta</i> or <i>frustums</i>) is the portion of a <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polyhedron"" title=""Polyhedron"">solid</a> (normally a <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cone_(geometry)"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Cone (geometry)"">cone</a> or <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pyramid_(geometry)"" title=""Pyramid (geometry)"">pyramid</a>) that lies between one or two <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Parallel_planes"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Parallel planes"">parallel planes</a> cutting it. A <b>right frustum</b> is a parallel <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Truncation_(geometry)"" title=""Truncation (geometry)"">truncation</a> of a <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Right_pyramid"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Right pyramid"">right pyramid</a> or right cone.<sup id=""cite_ref-45"" class=""reference""><a href=""#cite_note-45"">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup></dd>
<dt class=""glossary"" id=""function"" style=""margin-top: 0.4em;""><dfn class=""glossary""><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Function_(mathematics)"" title=""Function (mathematics)"">Function</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class=""glossary"">Is a process or a relation that associates each element <span class=""texhtml mvar"" style=""font-style:italic;"">x</span> of a <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Set_(mathematics)"" title=""Set (mathematics)"">set</a> <span class=""texhtml mvar"" style=""font-style:italic;"">X</span>,  the <i><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Domain_of_a_function"" title=""Domain of a function"">domain</a></i> of the function, to a single element <span class=""texhtml mvar"" style=""font-style:italic;"">y</span> of another set <span class=""texhtml mvar"" style=""font-style:italic;"">Y</span> (possibly the same set), the <i>codomain</i> of the function. If the function is called <span class=""texhtml mvar"" style=""font-style:italic;"">f</span>, this relation is denoted <span class=""texhtml""><i>y</i> = <i>f</i><span class=""nowrap"">&#8202;</span>(<i>x</i>)</span> (read <span class=""texhtml mvar"" style=""font-style:italic;"">f</span> of <span class=""texhtml mvar"" style=""font-style:italic;"">x</span>), the element <span class=""texhtml mvar"" style=""font-style:italic;"">x</span> is the <i><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Argument_of_a_function"" title=""Argument of a function"">argument</a></i> or <i>input</i> of the function, and <span class=""texhtml mvar"" style=""font-style:italic;"">y</span> is the <i>value of the function</i>, the <i>output</i>, or the <i>image</i> of <span class=""texhtml mvar"" style=""font-style:italic;"">x</span> by <span class=""texhtml mvar"" style=""font-style:italic;"">f</span>.<sup id=""cite_ref-MacLane_46-0"" class=""reference""><a href=""#cite_note-MacLane-46"">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> The symbol that is used for representing the input is the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Variable_(mathematics)"" title=""Variable (mathematics)"">variable</a> of the function (one often says that <span class=""texhtml mvar"" style=""font-style:italic;"">f</span> is a function of the variable <span class=""texhtml mvar"" style=""font-style:italic;"">x</span>).</dd>
<dt class=""glossary"" id=""function_composition"" style=""margin-top: 0.4em;""><dfn class=""glossary""><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Function_composition"" title=""Function composition"">Function composition</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class=""glossary"">Is an operation that takes two <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Function_(mathematics)"" title=""Function (mathematics)"">functions</a> <span class=""texhtml""><i>f</i></span> and <span class=""texhtml""><i>g</i></span> and produces a function <span class=""texhtml""><i>h</i></span> such that <span class=""texhtml""><i>h</i>(<i>x</i>) = <i>g</i>(<i>f</i>(<i>x</i>))</span>. In this operation, the function <span class=""texhtml""><i>g</i></span> is <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Function_application"" title=""Function application"">applied</a> to the result of applying the function <span class=""texhtml""><i>f</i></span> to <span class=""texhtml""><i>x</i></span>. That is, the functions <span class=""texhtml""><i>f</i> : <i>X</i> → <i>Y</i></span> and <span class=""texhtml""><i>g</i> : <i>Y</i> → <i>Z</i></span> are <b>composed</b> to yield a function that maps <span class=""texhtml""><i>x</i></span> in <span class=""texhtml""><i>X</i></span> to <span class=""texhtml""><i>g</i>(<i>f</i>(<i>x</i>))</span> in <span class=""texhtml""><i>Z</i></span>.</dd>
<dt class=""glossary"" id=""fundamental_theorem_of_calculus"" style=""margin-top: 0.4em;""><dfn class=""glossary""><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fundamental_theorem_of_calculus"" title=""Fundamental theorem of calculus"">Fundamental theorem of calculus</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class=""glossary"">The <b>fundamental theorem of calculus</b> is a <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Theorem"" title=""Theorem"">theorem</a> that links the concept of <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Derivative"" title=""Derivative"">differentiating</a> a <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Function_(mathematics)"" title=""Function (mathematics)"">function</a> with the concept of <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Integral"" title=""Integral"">integrating</a> a function. The first part of the theorem, sometimes called the <b>first fundamental theorem of calculus</b>, states that one of the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Antiderivative"" title=""Antiderivative"">antiderivatives</a> (also called <i>indefinite integral</i>), say <i>F</i>, of some function <i>f</i> may be obtained as the integral of <i>f</i> with a variable bound of integration. This implies the existence of <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Antiderivative"" title=""Antiderivative"">antiderivatives</a> for <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Continuous_function"" title=""Continuous function"">continuous functions</a>.<sup id=""cite_ref-47"" class=""reference""><a href=""#cite_note-47"">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup>  Conversely, the second part of the theorem, sometimes called the <b>second fundamental theorem of calculus</b>, states that the integral of a function <i>f</i> over some interval can be computed by using any one, say <i>F</i>, of its infinitely many <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Antiderivative"" title=""Antiderivative"">antiderivatives</a>. This part of the theorem has key practical applications, because explicitly finding the antiderivative of a function by <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Symbolic_integration"" title=""Symbolic integration"">symbolic integration</a> avoids <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Numerical_integration"" title=""Numerical integration"">numerical integration</a> to compute integrals. This provides generally a better numerical accuracy.</dd>
<dt class=""glossary"" id=""general_leibniz_rule"" style=""margin-top: 0.4em;""><dfn class=""glossary""><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/General_Leibniz_rule"" title=""General Leibniz rule"">General Leibniz rule</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class=""glossary"">The <b>general Leibniz rule</b>,<sup id=""cite_ref-48"" class=""reference""><a href=""#cite_note-48"">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> named after <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gottfried_Wilhelm_Leibniz"" title=""Gottfried Wilhelm Leibniz"">Gottfried Wilhelm Leibniz</a>, generalizes the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Product_rule"" title=""Product rule"">product rule</a> (which is also known as ""Leibniz's rule"").  It states that if <span class=""mwe-math-element""><span class=""mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y"" style=""display: none;""><math xmlns=""http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML""  alttext=""{\displaystyle f}"">
  <semantics>

        <mi>f</mi>

    </mrow>
    <annotation encoding=""application/x-tex"">{\displaystyle f}</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math></span><img src=""https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/132e57acb643253e7810ee9702d9581f159a1c61"" class=""mwe-math-fallback-image-inline"" aria-hidden=""true"" style=""vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:1.279ex; height:2.509ex;"" alt=""f""/></span> and <span class=""mwe-math-element""><span class=""mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y"" style=""display: none;""><math xmlns=""http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML""  alttext=""{\displaystyle g}"">
  <semantics>
    <mrow class=""MJX-TeXAtom-ORD"">
      <mstyle displaystyle=""true"" scriptlevel=""0"">
        <mi>g</mi>
      </mstyle>
    </mrow>
    <annotation encoding=""application/x-tex"">{\displaystyle g}</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math></span><img src=""https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/d3556280e66fe2c0d0140df20935a6f057381d77"" class=""mwe-math-fallback-image-inline"" aria-hidden=""true"" style=""vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:1.116ex; height:2.009ex;"" alt=""g""/></span> are <span class=""mwe-math-element""><span class=""mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y"" style=""display: none;""><math xmlns=""http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML""  alttext=""{\displaystyle n}"">

<dl><dd><span class=""mwe-math-element""><span class=""mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y"" style=""display: none;""><math xmlns=""http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML""  alttext=""{\displaystyle (fg)^{(n)}=\sum _{k=0}^{n}{n \choose k}f^{(n-k)}g^{(k)},}"">

    <annotation encoding=""application/x-tex"">{\displaystyle (fg)^{(n)}=\sum _{k=0}^{n}{n \choose k}f^{(n-k)}g^{(k)},}</annotation>

where <span class=""mwe-math-element""><span class=""mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y"" style=""display: none;""><math xmlns=""http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML""  alttext=""{\displaystyle {n \choose k}={n! \over k!(n-k)!}}"">
  <semantics>
    <mrow class=""MJX-TeXAtom-ORD"">

            </mrow>
            <mfrac linethickness=""0"">
              <mi>n</mi>
              <mi>k</mi>
            </mfrac>
            <mrow class=""MJX-TeXAtom-CLOSE"">
              <mo maxsize=""2.047em"" minsize=""2.047em"">)</mo>
            </mrow>
          </mrow>
        </mrow>
        <mo>=</mo>
        <mrow class=""MJX-TeXAtom-ORD"">
          <mfrac>
            <mrow>
              <mi>n</mi>
              <mo>!</mo>
            </mrow>
            <mrow>

              <mo>!</mo>
              <mo stretchy=""false"">(</mo>

              <mi>k</mi>

              <mo>!</mo>
            </mrow>
          </mfrac>
        </mrow>
      </mstyle>
    </mrow>
    <annotation encoding=""application/x-tex"">{\displaystyle {n \choose k}={n! \over k!(n-k)!}}</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math></span><img src=""https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/2ed1bdd61047e98b30df11a23956723badc802bc"" class=""mwe-math-fallback-image-inline"" aria-hidden=""true"" style=""vertical-align: -2.671ex; width:18.511ex; height:6.343ex;"" alt=""{n \choose k}={n! \over k!(n-k)!}""/></span> is the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Binomial_coefficient"" title=""Binomial coefficient"">binomial coefficient</a> and <span class=""mwe-math-element""><span class=""mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y"" style=""display: none;""><math xmlns=""http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML""  alttext=""{\displaystyle f^{(0)}\equiv f.}"">

            <mn>0</mn>

          </mrow>

        <mo>&#x2261;<!-- ≡ --></mo>
        <mi>f</mi>
        <mo>.</mo>

    </mrow>
    <annotation encoding=""application/x-tex"">{\displaystyle f^{(0)}\equiv f.}</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math></span><img src=""https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/2330050abc564f048e15651f273829cbc00cfee9"" class=""mwe-math-fallback-image-inline"" aria-hidden=""true"" style=""vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:8.678ex; height:3.176ex;"" alt=""{\displaystyle f^{(0)}\equiv f.}""/></span>

This can be proved by using the product rule and <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mathematical_induction"" title=""Mathematical induction"">mathematical induction</a>.</dd>
<dt class=""glossary"" id=""global_maximum"" style=""margin-top: 0.4em;""><dfn class=""glossary""><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Maxima_and_minima"" title=""Maxima and minima"">Global maximum</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class=""glossary"">In <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mathematical_analysis"" title=""Mathematical analysis"">mathematical analysis</a>, the <b>maxima and minima</b> (the respective plurals of <b>maximum</b> and <b>minimum</b>) of a <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Function_(mathematics)"" title=""Function (mathematics)"">function</a>, known collectively as <b>extrema</b> (the plural of <b>extremum</b>), are the largest and smallest value of the function, either within a given range (the <b>local</b> or <b>relative</b> extrema) or on the entire <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Domain_of_a_function"" title=""Domain of a function"">domain of a function</a> (the <b>global</b> or <b>absolute</b> extrema).<sup id=""cite_ref-49"" class=""reference""><a href=""#cite_note-49"">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup><sup id=""cite_ref-50"" class=""reference""><a href=""#cite_note-50"">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup><sup id=""cite_ref-51"" class=""reference""><a href=""#cite_note-51"">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pierre_de_Fermat"" title=""Pierre de Fermat"">Pierre de Fermat</a> was one of the first mathematicians to propose a general technique, <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Adequality"" title=""Adequality"">adequality</a>, for finding the maxima and minima of functions.

As defined in <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Set_theory"" title=""Set theory"">set theory</a>, the maximum and minimum of a <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Set_(mathematics)"" title=""Set (mathematics)"">set</a> are the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Greatest_and_least_elements"" title=""Greatest and least elements"">greatest and least elements</a> in the set, respectively. Unbounded infinite sets, such as the set of <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Real_number"" title=""Real number"">real numbers</a>, have no minimum or maximum.</dd>
<dt class=""glossary"" id=""global_minimum"" style=""margin-top: 0.4em;""><dfn class=""glossary""><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Maxima_and_minima"" title=""Maxima and minima"">Global minimum</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class=""glossary"">In <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mathematical_analysis"" title=""Mathematical analysis"">mathematical analysis</a>, the <b>maxima and minima</b> (the respective plurals of <b>maximum</b> and <b>minimum</b>) of a <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Function_(mathematics)"" title=""Function (mathematics)"">function</a>, known collectively as <b>extrema</b> (the plural of <b>extremum</b>), are the largest and smallest value of the function, either within a given range (the <b>local</b> or <b>relative</b> extrema) or on the entire <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Domain_of_a_function"" title=""Domain of a function"">domain of a function</a> (the <b>global</b> or <b>absolute</b> extrema).<sup id=""cite_ref-52"" class=""reference""><a href=""#cite_note-52"">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup><sup id=""cite_ref-53"" class=""reference""><a href=""#cite_note-53"">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup><sup id=""cite_ref-54"" class=""reference""><a href=""#cite_note-54"">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pierre_de_Fermat"" title=""Pierre de Fermat"">Pierre de Fermat</a> was one of the first mathematicians to propose a general technique, <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Adequality"" title=""Adequality"">adequality</a>, for finding the maxima and minima of functions.

As defined in <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Set_theory"" title=""Set theory"">set theory</a>, the maximum and minimum of a <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Set_(mathematics)"" title=""Set (mathematics)"">set</a> are the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Greatest_and_least_elements"" title=""Greatest and least elements"">greatest and least elements</a> in the set, respectively. Unbounded infinite sets, such as the set of <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Real_number"" title=""Real number"">real numbers</a>, have no minimum or maximum.</dd>
<dt class=""glossary"" id=""golden_spiral"" style=""margin-top: 0.4em;""><dfn class=""glossary""><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Golden_spiral"" title=""Golden spiral"">Golden spiral</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class=""glossary"">In <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Geometry"" title=""Geometry"">geometry</a>, a <b>golden spiral</b> is a <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Logarithmic_spiral"" title=""Logarithmic spiral"">logarithmic spiral</a> whose growth factor is <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Phi"" title=""Phi""><span class=""texhtml"">&#966;</span></a>, the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Golden_ratio"" title=""Golden ratio"">golden ratio</a>.<sup id=""cite_ref-55"" class=""reference""><a href=""#cite_note-55"">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> That is, a golden spiral gets wider (or further from its origin) by a factor of <span class=""texhtml"">&#966;</span> for every quarter turn it makes.</dd>
<dt class=""glossary"" id=""gradient"" style=""margin-top: 0.4em;""><dfn class=""glossary""><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gradient"" title=""Gradient"">Gradient</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class=""glossary"">Is a multi-variable generalization of the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Derivative"" title=""Derivative"">derivative</a>.  While a derivative can be defined on functions of a single variable, for <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Function_of_several_variables"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Function of several variables"">functions of several variables</a>, the gradient takes its place.  The gradient is a <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vector-valued_function"" title=""Vector-valued function"">vector-valued function</a>, as opposed to a derivative, which is <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Scalar-valued_function"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Scalar-valued function"">scalar-valued</a>.</dd>
<dt class=""glossary"" id=""harmonic_progression"" style=""margin-top: 0.4em;""><dfn class=""glossary""><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Harmonic_progression_(mathematics)"" title=""Harmonic progression (mathematics)"">Harmonic progression</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class=""glossary"">In <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mathematics"" title=""Mathematics"">mathematics</a>, a <b>harmonic progression</b> (or <b>harmonic sequence</b>) is a progression formed by taking the reciprocals of an <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arithmetic_progression"" title=""Arithmetic progression"">arithmetic progression</a>. It is a <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sequence"" title=""Sequence"">sequence</a> of the form

<dl><dd><span class=""mwe-math-element""><span class=""mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y"" style=""display: none;""><math xmlns=""http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML""  alttext=""{\displaystyle {\frac {1}{a}},\ {\frac {1}{a+d}}\ ,{\frac {1}{a+2d}}\ ,{\frac {1}{a+3d}}\ ,\cdots ,{\frac {1}{a+kd}},}"">
  <semantics>
    <mrow class=""MJX-TeXAtom-ORD"">

        <mrow class=""MJX-TeXAtom-ORD"">

        </mrow>

              <mi>a</mi>
              <mo>+</mo>
              <mi>d</mi>

              <mi>a</mi>

  </semantics>
</math></span><img src=""https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/eb3d0a8448a2b942e3b1886a90ce71ea096c4a7c"" class=""mwe-math-fallback-image-inline"" aria-hidden=""true"" style=""vertical-align: -2.171ex; width:41.34ex; height:5.509ex;"" alt=""{\displaystyle {\frac {1}{a}},\ {\frac {1}{a+d}}\ ,{\frac {1}{a+2d}}\ ,{\frac {1}{a+3d}}\ ,\cdots ,{\frac {1}{a+kd}},}""/></span></dd></dl>

where &#8722;a/<i>d</i> is not a <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Natural_number"" title=""Natural number"">natural number</a> and <i>k</i> <b>is</b> a natural number.

Equivalently, a sequence is a harmonic progression when each term is the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Harmonic_mean"" title=""Harmonic mean"">harmonic mean</a> of the neighboring terms.

It is not possible for a harmonic progression (other than the trivial case where <i>a</i> = 1 and <i>k</i> = 0)  to sum to an <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Integer"" title=""Integer"">integer</a>. The reason is that, necessarily, at least one denominator of the progression will be divisible by a <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number"" title=""Prime number"">prime number</a> that does not divide any other denominator.<sup id=""cite_ref-56"" class=""reference""><a href=""#cite_note-56"">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup></dd>

<dt class=""glossary"" id=""higher_derivative"" style=""margin-top: 0.4em;""><dfn class=""glossary""><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Derivative#Higher_derivatives"" title=""Derivative"">Higher derivative</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class=""glossary"">Let <span class=""texhtml""><i>f</i></span> be a differentiable function, and let <span class=""texhtml""><i>f</i> ′</span> be its derivative. The derivative of <span class=""texhtml""><i>f</i> ′</span> (if it has one) is written <span class=""texhtml""><i>f</i> ′′</span> and is called the <i><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Second_derivative"" title=""Second derivative"">second derivative</a> of <span class=""texhtml"">f</span></i>.  Similarly, the derivative of the second derivative, if it exists, is written <span class=""texhtml""><i>f</i> ′′′</span> and is called the <i><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Third_derivative"" title=""Third derivative"">third derivative</a> of <span class=""texhtml"">f</span></i>. Continuing this process, one can define, if it exists, the <span class=""texhtml""><i>n</i></span>th derivative as the derivative of the <span class=""texhtml"">(<i>n</i>-1)</span>th derivative. These repeated derivatives are called <i>higher-order derivatives</i>. The <span class=""texhtml""><i>n</i></span>th derivative is also called the <b>derivative of order <span class=""texhtml""><i>n</i></span></b>.</dd>
<dt class=""glossary"" id=""homogeneous_linear_differential_equation"" style=""margin-top: 0.4em;""><dfn class=""glossary""><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Homogeneous_differential_equation"" title=""Homogeneous differential equation"">Homogeneous linear differential equation</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class=""glossary"">A <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Differential_equation"" title=""Differential equation"">differential equation</a> can be <b>homogeneous</b> in either of two respects.

A <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/First_order_differential_equation"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""First order differential equation"">first order differential equation</a> is said to be homogeneous if it may be written
<dl><dd><span class=""mwe-math-element""><span class=""mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y"" style=""display: none;""><math xmlns=""http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML""  alttext=""{\displaystyle f(x,y)dy=g(x,y)dx,}"">
  <semantics>
    <mrow class=""MJX-TeXAtom-ORD"">
      <mstyle displaystyle=""true"" scriptlevel=""0"">
        <mi>f</mi>
        <mo stretchy=""false"">(</mo>
        <mi>x</mi>
        <mo>,</mo>
        <mi>y</mi>
        <mo stretchy=""false"">)</mo>
        <mi>d</mi>
        <mi>y</mi>
        <mo>=</mo>
        <mi>g</mi>
        <mo stretchy=""false"">(</mo>
        <mi>x</mi>
        <mo>,</mo>
        <mi>y</mi>
        <mo stretchy=""false"">)</mo>
        <mi>d</mi>
        <mi>x</mi>
        <mo>,</mo>
      </mstyle>
    </mrow>
    <annotation encoding=""application/x-tex"">{\displaystyle f(x,y)dy=g(x,y)dx,}</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math></span><img src=""https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/d4b0be82f9ea4a314c2c3adad396acb548c7adb5"" class=""mwe-math-fallback-image-inline"" aria-hidden=""true"" style=""vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:21.714ex; height:2.843ex;"" alt=""{\displaystyle f(x,y)dy=g(x,y)dx,}""/></span></dd></dl>
where <span class=""texhtml mvar"" style=""font-style:italic;"">f</span> and <span class=""texhtml mvar"" style=""font-style:italic;"">g</span> are <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Homogeneous_function"" title=""Homogeneous function"">homogeneous functions</a> of the same degree of <span class=""texhtml mvar"" style=""font-style:italic;"">x</span> and <span class=""texhtml mvar"" style=""font-style:italic;"">y</span>. In this case, the change of variable <span class=""texhtml""><i>y</i> = <i>ux</i></span> leads to an equation of the form
<dl><dd><span class=""mwe-math-element""><span class=""mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y"" style=""display: none;""><math xmlns=""http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML""  alttext=""{\displaystyle {\frac {dx}{x}}=h(u)du,}"">
  <semantics>
    <mrow class=""MJX-TeXAtom-ORD"">
      <mstyle displaystyle=""true"" scriptlevel=""0"">
        <mrow class=""MJX-TeXAtom-ORD"">
          <mfrac>
            <mrow>
              <mi>d</mi>
              <mi>x</mi>
            </mrow>
            <mi>x</mi>
          </mfrac>
        </mrow>
        <mo>=</mo>
        <mi>h</mi>
        <mo stretchy=""false"">(</mo>
        <mi>u</mi>
        <mo stretchy=""false"">)</mo>
        <mi>d</mi>
        <mi>u</mi>
        <mo>,</mo>
      </mstyle>
    </mrow>
    <annotation encoding=""application/x-tex"">{\displaystyle {\frac {dx}{x}}=h(u)du,}</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math></span><img src=""https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/655ac39b47bf14555c0efeb383326d24ac558657"" class=""mwe-math-fallback-image-inline"" aria-hidden=""true"" style=""vertical-align: -1.838ex; width:14.151ex; height:5.343ex;"" alt=""{\displaystyle {\frac {dx}{x}}=h(u)du,}""/></span></dd></dl>
which is easy to solve by <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Integral"" title=""Integral"">integration</a> of the two members.

Otherwise, a differential equation is homogeneous if it is a homogeneous function of the unknown function and its derivatives. In the case of <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Linear_differential_equation"" title=""Linear differential equation"">linear differential equations</a>, this means that there are no constant terms. The solutions of any linear <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ordinary_differential_equation"" title=""Ordinary differential equation"">ordinary differential equation</a> of any order may be deduced by integration from the solution of the homogeneous equation obtained by removing the constant term.</dd>
<dt class=""glossary"" id=""hyperbolic_function"" style=""margin-top: 0.4em;""><dfn class=""glossary""><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hyperbolic_function"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Hyperbolic function"">Hyperbolic function</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class=""glossary""><b>Hyperbolic functions</b> are analogs of the ordinary <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Trigonometric_function"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Trigonometric function"">trigonometric</a>, or <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Unit_circle"" title=""Unit circle"">circular</a>, functions.</dd>

<dt class=""glossary"" id=""identity_function"" style=""margin-top: 0.4em;""><dfn class=""glossary""><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Identity_function"" title=""Identity function"">Identity function</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class=""glossary"">Also called an <b>identity relation</b> or <b>identity map</b> or <b>identity transformation</b>, is a <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Function_(mathematics)"" title=""Function (mathematics)"">function</a> that always returns the same value that was used as its argument. In <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Equation"" title=""Equation"">equations</a>, the function is given by <span class=""texhtml""><i>f</i>(<i>x</i>) = <i>x</i></span>.</dd>
<dt class=""glossary"" id=""imaginary_number"" style=""margin-top: 0.4em;""><dfn class=""glossary""><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Imaginary_number"" title=""Imaginary number"">Imaginary number</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class=""glossary"">Is a <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Complex_number"" title=""Complex number"">complex number</a> that can be written as a <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Real_number"" title=""Real number"">real number</a> multiplied by the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Imaginary_unit"" title=""Imaginary unit"">imaginary unit</a> <span class=""texhtml mvar"" style=""font-style:italic;"">i</span>,<sup id=""cite_ref-57"" class=""reference""><a href=""#cite_note-57"">&#91;note 2&#93;</a></sup> which is defined by its property <span class=""texhtml""><i>i</i><sup>2</sup> = −1</span>.<sup id=""cite_ref-58"" class=""reference""><a href=""#cite_note-58"">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> The <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Square_(algebra)"" title=""Square (algebra)"">square</a> of an imaginary number <span class=""texhtml mvar"" style=""font-style:italic;"">bi</span> is <span class=""texhtml"">−<i>b</i><sup>2</sup></span>. For example, <span class=""texhtml"">5<i>i</i></span> is an imaginary number, and its square is <span class=""texhtml"">−25</span>. Zero is considered to be both real and imaginary.<sup id=""cite_ref-59"" class=""reference""><a href=""#cite_note-59"">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup></dd>
<dt class=""glossary"" id=""implicit_function"" style=""margin-top: 0.4em;""><dfn class=""glossary""><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Implicit_function"" title=""Implicit function"">Implicit function</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class=""glossary"">In <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mathematics"" title=""Mathematics"">mathematics</a>, an implicit equation is a <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Relation_(mathematics)"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Relation (mathematics)"">relation</a>  of the form <span class=""mwe-math-element""><span class=""mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y"" style=""display: none;""><math xmlns=""http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML""  alttext=""{\displaystyle R(x_{1},\ldots ,x_{n})=0}"">
  <semantics>
    <mrow class=""MJX-TeXAtom-ORD"">
      <mstyle displaystyle=""true"" scriptlevel=""0"">
        <mi>R</mi>
        <mo stretchy=""false"">(</mo>
        <msub>
          <mi>x</mi>
          <mrow class=""MJX-TeXAtom-ORD"">
            <mn>1</mn>
          </mrow>
        </msub>
        <mo>,</mo>
        <mo>&#x2026;<!-- … --></mo>
        <mo>,</mo>
        <msub>
          <mi>x</mi>
          <mrow class=""MJX-TeXAtom-ORD"">
            <mi>n</mi>
          </mrow>
        </msub>
        <mo stretchy=""false"">)</mo>
        <mo>=</mo>
        <mn>0</mn>
      </mstyle>
    </mrow>
    <annotation encoding=""application/x-tex"">{\displaystyle R(x_{1},\ldots ,x_{n})=0}</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math></span><img src=""https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/2da5e7cf8f171a3430f3a4a66679be85e22dcbc4"" class=""mwe-math-fallback-image-inline"" aria-hidden=""true"" style=""vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:17.945ex; height:2.843ex;"" alt=""{\displaystyle R(x_{1},\ldots ,x_{n})=0}""/></span>, where <span class=""mwe-math-element""><span class=""mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y"" style=""display: none;""><math xmlns=""http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML""  alttext=""{\displaystyle R}"">
  <semantics>
    <mrow class=""MJX-TeXAtom-ORD"">
      <mstyle displaystyle=""true"" scriptlevel=""0"">
        <mi>R</mi>
      </mstyle>
    </mrow>
    <annotation encoding=""application/x-tex"">{\displaystyle R}</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math></span><img src=""https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/4b0bfb3769bf24d80e15374dc37b0441e2616e33"" class=""mwe-math-fallback-image-inline"" aria-hidden=""true"" style=""vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.764ex; height:2.176ex;"" alt=""R""/></span> is a <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Function_(mathematics)"" title=""Function (mathematics)"">function</a> of several variables (often a <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polynomial"" title=""Polynomial"">polynomial</a>). For example, the implicit equation of the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Unit_circle"" title=""Unit circle"">unit circle</a> is <span class=""mwe-math-element""><span class=""mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y"" style=""display: none;""><math xmlns=""http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML""  alttext=""{\displaystyle x^{2}+y^{2}-1=0}"">
  <semantics>
    <mrow class=""MJX-TeXAtom-ORD"">
      <mstyle displaystyle=""true"" scriptlevel=""0"">

          <mrow class=""MJX-TeXAtom-ORD"">
            <mn>2</mn>
          </mrow>
        </msup>
        <mo>+</mo>
        <msup>
          <mi>y</mi>
          <mrow class=""MJX-TeXAtom-ORD"">

          </mrow>

        <mo>&#x2212;<!-- − --></mo>
        <mn>1</mn>
        <mo>=</mo>
        <mn>0</mn>
      </mstyle>
    </mrow>
    <annotation encoding=""application/x-tex"">{\displaystyle x^{2}+y^{2}-1=0}</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math></span><img src=""https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/1ee594b8851d760d0e2d44aba714907aca657b8f"" class=""mwe-math-fallback-image-inline"" aria-hidden=""true"" style=""vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:15.703ex; height:3.009ex;"" alt=""{\displaystyle x^{2}+y^{2}-1=0}""/></span>.

An <b>implicit function</b> is a <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Function_(mathematics)"" title=""Function (mathematics)"">function</a> that is defined implicitly by an implicit equation, by associating one of the variables (the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Value_(mathematics)"" title=""Value (mathematics)"">value</a>) with the others (the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Argument_of_a_function"" title=""Argument of a function"">arguments</a>).<sup id=""cite_ref-Chiang_60-0"" class=""reference""><a href=""#cite_note-Chiang-60"">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup><sup class=""reference"" style=""white-space:nowrap;"">:<span>204–206</span></sup> Thus, an implicit function for <span class=""mwe-math-element""><span class=""mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y"" style=""display: none;""><math xmlns=""http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML""  alttext=""{\displaystyle y}"">
  <semantics>
    <mrow class=""MJX-TeXAtom-ORD"">
      <mstyle displaystyle=""true"" scriptlevel=""0"">
        <mi>y</mi>
      </mstyle>
    </mrow>
    <annotation encoding=""application/x-tex"">{\displaystyle y}</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math></span><img src=""https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/b8a6208ec717213d4317e666f1ae872e00620a0d"" class=""mwe-math-fallback-image-inline"" aria-hidden=""true"" style=""vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:1.155ex; height:2.009ex;"" alt=""y""/></span> in the context of the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Unit_circle"" title=""Unit circle"">unit circle</a> is defined implicitly by <span class=""mwe-math-element""><span class=""mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y"" style=""display: none;""><math xmlns=""http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML""  alttext=""{\displaystyle x^{2}+f(x)^{2}-1=0}"">

This implicit equation defines <span class=""mwe-math-element""><span class=""mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y"" style=""display: none;""><math xmlns=""http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML""  alttext=""{\displaystyle f}"">

The <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Implicit_function_theorem"" title=""Implicit function theorem"">implicit function theorem</a> provides conditions under which some kinds of relations define an implicit function, namely relations defined as the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Indicator_function"" title=""Indicator function"">indicator function</a> of the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zero_set"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Zero set"">zero set</a> of some <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Continuously_differentiable"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Continuously differentiable"">continuously differentiable</a> <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Multivariable_calculus"" title=""Multivariable calculus"">multivariate</a> function.</dd>
<dt class=""glossary"" id=""improper_fraction"" style=""margin-top: 0.4em;""><dfn class=""glossary""><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Improper_fraction"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Improper fraction"">Improper fraction</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class=""glossary"">Common fractions can be classified as either proper or improper. When the numerator and the denominator are both positive, the fraction is called proper if the numerator is less than the denominator, and improper otherwise.<sup id=""cite_ref-61"" class=""reference""><a href=""#cite_note-61"">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup><sup id=""cite_ref-62"" class=""reference""><a href=""#cite_note-62"">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> In general, a common fraction is said to be a proper fraction if the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Absolute_value"" title=""Absolute value"">absolute value</a> of the fraction is strictly less than one—that is, if the fraction is greater than −1 and less than 1.<sup id=""cite_ref-63"" class=""reference""><a href=""#cite_note-63"">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup><sup id=""cite_ref-64"" class=""reference""><a href=""#cite_note-64"">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup>
It is said to be an improper fraction, or sometimes top-heavy fraction,<sup id=""cite_ref-65"" class=""reference""><a href=""#cite_note-65"">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> if the absolute value of the fraction is greater than or equal to 1. Examples of proper fractions are 2/3, –3/4, and 4/9; examples of improper fractions are 9/4, –4/3, and 3/3.</dd>
<dt class=""glossary"" id=""improper_integral"" style=""margin-top: 0.4em;""><dfn class=""glossary""><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Improper_integral"" title=""Improper integral"">Improper integral</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class=""glossary"">In <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mathematical_analysis"" title=""Mathematical analysis"">mathematical analysis</a>, an improper integral is the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Limit_(mathematics)"" title=""Limit (mathematics)"">limit</a> of a <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Definite_integral"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Definite integral"">definite integral</a> as an endpoint of the interval(s) of integration approaches either a specified <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Real_number"" title=""Real number"">real number</a>, <span class=""mwe-math-element""><span class=""mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y"" style=""display: none;""><math xmlns=""http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML""  alttext=""{\displaystyle \infty }"">
  <semantics>
    <mrow class=""MJX-TeXAtom-ORD"">
      <mstyle displaystyle=""true"" scriptlevel=""0"">

      </mstyle>
    </mrow>
    <annotation encoding=""application/x-tex"">{\displaystyle \infty }</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math></span><img src=""https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/c26c105004f30c27aa7c2a9c601550a4183b1f21"" class=""mwe-math-fallback-image-inline"" aria-hidden=""true"" style=""vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:2.324ex; height:1.676ex;"" alt=""\infty ""/></span>, <span class=""mwe-math-element""><span class=""mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y"" style=""display: none;""><math xmlns=""http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML""  alttext=""{\displaystyle -\infty }"">

Such an integral is often written symbolically just like a standard definite integral, in some cases with <i>infinity</i> as a limit of integration.

<dt class=""glossary"" id=""inflection_point"" style=""margin-top: 0.4em;""><dfn class=""glossary""><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Inflection_point"" title=""Inflection point"">Inflection point</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class=""glossary"">In <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Differential_calculus"" title=""Differential calculus"">differential calculus</a>, an <b>inflection point</b>, <b>point of inflection</b>, <b>flex</b>, or <b>inflection</b> (British English: <b>inflexion</b>) is a point on a <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Continuous_function"" title=""Continuous function"">continuous</a> <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Plane_curve"" title=""Plane curve"">plane curve</a> at which the curve changes from being <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Concave_function"" title=""Concave function"">concave</a> (concave downward) to <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Convex_function"" title=""Convex function"">convex</a> (concave upward), or vice versa.</dd>
<dt class=""glossary"" id=""instantaneous_rate_of_change"" style=""margin-top: 0.4em;""><dfn class=""glossary""><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Derivative"" title=""Derivative"">Instantaneous rate of change</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class=""glossary"">The derivative of a function of a single variable at a chosen input value, when it exists, is the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Slope"" title=""Slope"">slope</a> of the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tangent"" title=""Tangent"">tangent line</a> to the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graph_of_a_function"" title=""Graph of a function"">graph of the function</a> at that point. The tangent line is the best <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Linear_approximation"" title=""Linear approximation"">linear approximation</a> of the function near that input value.  For this reason, the derivative is often described as the ""instantaneous rate of change"", the ratio of the instantaneous change in the dependent variable to that of the independent variable. .</dd>
<dt class=""glossary"" id=""instantaneous_velocity"" style=""margin-top: 0.4em;""><dfn class=""glossary""><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Velocity#Instantaneous_velocity"" title=""Velocity"">Instantaneous velocity</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class=""glossary"">If we consider <span class=""texhtml""><i><b>v</b></i></span> as velocity and <span class=""texhtml""><i><b>x</b></i></span> as the displacement (change in position) vector, then we can express the (instantaneous) velocity of a particle or object, at any particular time <span class=""texhtml""><i>t</i></span>, as the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Derivative"" title=""Derivative"">derivative</a> of the position with respect to time:

<dl><dd><span class=""mwe-math-element""><span class=""mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y"" style=""display: none;""><math xmlns=""http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML""  alttext=""{\displaystyle {\boldsymbol {v}}=\lim _{{\Delta t}\to 0}{\frac {\Delta {\boldsymbol {x}}}{\Delta t}}={\frac {d{\boldsymbol {x}}}{d{\mathit {t}}}}.}"">
  <semantics>
    <mrow class=""MJX-TeXAtom-ORD"">
      <mstyle displaystyle=""true"" scriptlevel=""0"">
        <mrow class=""MJX-TeXAtom-ORD"">
          <mi mathvariant=""bold-italic"">v</mi>
        </mrow>
        <mo>=</mo>
        <munder>
          <mo movablelimits=""true"" form=""prefix"">lim</mo>
          <mrow class=""MJX-TeXAtom-ORD"">
            <mrow class=""MJX-TeXAtom-ORD"">
              <mi mathvariant=""normal"">&#x0394;<!-- Δ --></mi>
              <mi>t</mi>
            </mrow>
            <mo stretchy=""false"">&#x2192;<!-- → --></mo>
            <mn>0</mn>
          </mrow>
        </munder>
        <mrow class=""MJX-TeXAtom-ORD"">
          <mfrac>
            <mrow>
              <mi mathvariant=""normal"">&#x0394;<!-- Δ --></mi>
              <mrow class=""MJX-TeXAtom-ORD"">
                <mi mathvariant=""bold-italic"">x</mi>
              </mrow>
            </mrow>
            <mrow>
              <mi mathvariant=""normal"">&#x0394;<!-- Δ --></mi>
              <mi>t</mi>
            </mrow>
          </mfrac>
        </mrow>
        <mo>=</mo>
        <mrow class=""MJX-TeXAtom-ORD"">
          <mfrac>
            <mrow>
              <mi>d</mi>
              <mrow class=""MJX-TeXAtom-ORD"">
                <mi mathvariant=""bold-italic"">x</mi>
              </mrow>
            </mrow>
            <mrow>
              <mi>d</mi>
              <mrow class=""MJX-TeXAtom-ORD"">
                <mrow class=""MJX-TeXAtom-ORD"">
                  <mi class=""MJX-tex-mathit"" mathvariant=""italic"">t</mi>
                </mrow>
              </mrow>
            </mrow>
          </mfrac>
        </mrow>
        <mo>.</mo>
      </mstyle>
    </mrow>
    <annotation encoding=""application/x-tex"">{\displaystyle {\boldsymbol {v}}=\lim _{{\Delta t}\to 0}{\frac {\Delta {\boldsymbol {x}}}{\Delta t}}={\frac {d{\boldsymbol {x}}}{d{\mathit {t}}}}.}</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math></span><img src=""https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/98326656eb93e2b598177b2a80ff949240888f99"" class=""mwe-math-fallback-image-inline"" aria-hidden=""true"" style=""vertical-align: -2.171ex; width:20.864ex; height:5.676ex;"" alt=""{\boldsymbol {v}}=\lim _{{\Delta t}\to 0}{\frac {\Delta {\boldsymbol {x}}}{\Delta t}}={\frac {d{\boldsymbol {x}}}{d{\mathit {t}}}}.""/></span></dd></dl>

From this derivative equation, in the one-dimensional case it can be seen that the area under a velocity vs. time (<span class=""texhtml""><i><b>v</b></i></span> vs. <span class=""texhtml""><i>t</i></span> graph) is the displacement, <span class=""texhtml""><i><b>x</b></i></span>. In calculus terms, the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Integral"" title=""Integral"">integral</a> of the velocity function <span class=""texhtml""><i><b>v</b></i>(<i>t</i>)</span> is the displacement function <span class=""texhtml""><i><b>x</b></i>(<i>t</i>)</span>. In the figure, this corresponds to the yellow area under the curve labeled <span class=""texhtml""><i><b>s</b></i></span> (<span class=""texhtml""><i><b>s</b></i></span> being an alternative notation for displacement).

<dl><dd><span class=""mwe-math-element""><span class=""mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y"" style=""display: none;""><math xmlns=""http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML""  alttext=""{\displaystyle {\boldsymbol {x}}=\int {\boldsymbol {v}}\ d{\mathit {t}}.}"">
  <semantics>
    <mrow class=""MJX-TeXAtom-ORD"">
      <mstyle displaystyle=""true"" scriptlevel=""0"">
        <mrow class=""MJX-TeXAtom-ORD"">
          <mi mathvariant=""bold-italic"">x</mi>
        </mrow>
        <mo>=</mo>
        <mo>&#x222B;<!-- ∫ --></mo>
        <mrow class=""MJX-TeXAtom-ORD"">
          <mi mathvariant=""bold-italic"">v</mi>
        </mrow>
        <mtext>&#xA0;</mtext>
        <mi>d</mi>
        <mrow class=""MJX-TeXAtom-ORD"">
          <mrow class=""MJX-TeXAtom-ORD"">
            <mi class=""MJX-tex-mathit"" mathvariant=""italic"">t</mi>
          </mrow>
        </mrow>
        <mo>.</mo>
      </mstyle>
    </mrow>
    <annotation encoding=""application/x-tex"">{\displaystyle {\boldsymbol {x}}=\int {\boldsymbol {v}}\ d{\mathit {t}}.}</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math></span><img src=""https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/dfc65107366d82be9fb36c9d2c13a1bb91405838"" class=""mwe-math-fallback-image-inline"" aria-hidden=""true"" style=""vertical-align: -2.338ex; width:11.745ex; height:5.676ex;"" alt=""{\displaystyle {\boldsymbol {x}}=\int {\boldsymbol {v}}\ d{\mathit {t}}.}""/></span></dd></dl>

Since the derivative of the position with respect to time gives the change in position (in <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Metre"" title=""Metre"">metres</a>) divided by the change in time (in <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Second"" title=""Second"">seconds</a>), velocity is measured in <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Metre_per_second"" title=""Metre per second"">metres per second</a> (m/s). Although the concept of an instantaneous velocity might at first seem counter-intuitive, it may be thought of as the velocity that the object would continue to travel at if it stopped accelerating at that moment. .</dd>
<dt class=""glossary"" id=""integral"" style=""margin-top: 0.4em;""><dfn class=""glossary""><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Integral"" title=""Integral"">Integral</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class=""glossary"">An integral assigns numbers to functions in a way that can describe displacement, area, volume, and other concepts that arise by combining <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Infinitesimal"" title=""Infinitesimal"">infinitesimal</a> data. Integration is one of the two main operations of calculus, with its inverse operation, <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Derivative"" title=""Derivative"">differentiation</a>, being the other. .</dd>
<dt class=""glossary"" id=""integral_symbol"" style=""margin-top: 0.4em;""><dfn class=""glossary""><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Integral_symbol"" title=""Integral symbol"">Integral symbol</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class=""glossary"">The integral symbol:
<dl><dd><span style=""font-size:200%"">∫</span> (<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Unicode"" title=""Unicode"">Unicode</a>), <span class=""mwe-math-element""><span class=""mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y"" style=""display: none;""><math xmlns=""http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML""  alttext=""{\displaystyle \displaystyle \int }"">
  <semantics>

is used to denote <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Integral"" title=""Integral"">integrals</a> and <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Antiderivatives"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Antiderivatives"">antiderivatives</a> in <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mathematics"" title=""Mathematics"">mathematics</a>. .</dd>
<dt class=""glossary"" id=""integrand"" style=""margin-top: 0.4em;""><dfn class=""glossary""><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Integrand"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Integrand"">Integrand</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class=""glossary"">The function to be integrated in an integral.</dd>
<dt class=""glossary"" id=""integration_by_parts"" style=""margin-top: 0.4em;""><dfn class=""glossary""><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Integration_by_parts"" title=""Integration by parts"">Integration by parts</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class=""glossary"">In calculus, and more generally in <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mathematical_analysis"" title=""Mathematical analysis"">mathematical analysis</a>, <b>integration by parts</b> or <b>partial integration</b> is a process that finds the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Integral_(mathematics)"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Integral (mathematics)"">integral</a> of a <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Product_(mathematics)"" title=""Product (mathematics)"">product</a> of functions in terms of the integral of their derivative and antiderivative. It is frequently used to transform the antiderivative of a product of functions into an antiderivative for which a solution can be more easily found. The rule can be readily derived by integrating the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Product_rule"" title=""Product rule"">product rule</a> of <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Derivative"" title=""Derivative"">differentiation</a>.

If <span class=""texhtml""><i>u</i> = <i>u</i>(<i>x</i>)</span> and <span class=""texhtml""><i>du</i> = <i>u<span class=""nowrap"" style=""padding-left:0.05em;"">′</span></i>(<i>x</i>) <i>dx</i></span>, while <span class=""texhtml""><i>v</i> = <i>v</i>(<i>x</i>)</span> and <span class=""texhtml""><i>dv</i> = <i>v<span class=""nowrap"" style=""padding-left:0.1em;"">′</span></i>(<i>x</i>) <i>dx</i></span>, then integration by parts states that:

or more compactly:

<dl><dd><span class=""mwe-math-element""><span class=""mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y"" style=""display: none;""><math xmlns=""http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML""  alttext=""{\displaystyle \int u\,dv=uv-\int v\,du.}"">

    <annotation encoding=""application/x-tex"">{\displaystyle \int u\,dv=uv-\int v\,du.}</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math></span><img src=""https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/7543879c4840a6b9d2a9ae0846cd7b30360d59b8"" class=""mwe-math-fallback-image-inline"" aria-hidden=""true"" style=""vertical-align: -2.338ex; width:22.325ex; height:5.676ex;"" alt=""{\displaystyle \int u\,dv=uv-\int v\,du.}""/></span></dd></dl>

Mathematician <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Brook_Taylor"" title=""Brook Taylor"">Brook Taylor</a> discovered integration by parts, first publishing the idea in <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1715_in_science"" title=""1715 in science"">1715</a>.<sup id=""cite_ref-Brook_Taylor_biography,_St._Andrews_66-0"" class=""reference""><a href=""#cite_note-Brook_Taylor_biography,_St._Andrews-66"">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup><sup id=""cite_ref-Brook_Taylor_biography,_Stetson_67-0"" class=""reference""><a href=""#cite_note-Brook_Taylor_biography,_Stetson-67"">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> More general formulations of integration by parts exist for the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Riemann%E2%80%93Stieltjes_integral#Properties_and_relation_to_the_Riemann_integral"" title=""Riemann–Stieltjes integral"">Riemann–Stieltjes</a> and <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lebesgue%E2%80%93Stieltjes_integral#Integration_by_parts"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Lebesgue–Stieltjes integral"">Lebesgue–Stieltjes integrals</a>. The discrete analogue for sequences is called <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Summation_by_parts"" title=""Summation by parts"">summation by parts</a>.
 .</dd>
<dt class=""glossary"" id=""integration_by_substitution"" style=""margin-top: 0.4em;""><dfn class=""glossary""><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Integration_by_substitution"" title=""Integration by substitution"">Integration by substitution</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class=""glossary"">Also known as <i>u</i>-substitution, is a method for solving <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Integral"" title=""Integral"">integrals</a>. Using the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fundamental_theorem_of_calculus"" title=""Fundamental theorem of calculus"">fundamental theorem of calculus</a> often requires finding an <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Antiderivative"" title=""Antiderivative"">antiderivative</a>. For this and other reasons, integration by substitution is an important tool in mathematics. It is the counterpart to the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chain_rule"" title=""Chain rule"">chain rule</a> for <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Derivative"" title=""Derivative"">differentiation</a>. .</dd>
<dt class=""glossary"" id=""intermediate_value_theorem"" style=""margin-top: 0.4em;""><dfn class=""glossary""><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Intermediate_value_theorem"" title=""Intermediate value theorem"">Intermediate value theorem</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class=""glossary"">In <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mathematical_analysis"" title=""Mathematical analysis"">mathematical analysis</a>, the intermediate value theorem states that if a <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Continuous_function"" title=""Continuous function"">continuous function</a>, <i>f</i>, with an <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Interval_(mathematics)"" title=""Interval (mathematics)"">interval</a>, [<i>a</i>, <i>b</i>], as its <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Domain_of_a_function"" title=""Domain of a function"">domain</a>, takes values <i>f</i>(<i>a</i>) and <i>f</i>(<i>b</i>) at each end of the interval, then it also takes any value between <i>f</i>(<i>a</i>) and <i>f</i>(<i>b</i>) at some point within the interval.

This has two important <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Corollary"" title=""Corollary"">corollaries</a>:

<ol><li>If a continuous function has values of opposite sign inside an interval, then it has a root in that interval (<b>Bolzano's theorem</b>).<sup id=""cite_ref-68"" class=""reference""><a href=""#cite_note-68"">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>The <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Image_(mathematics)"" title=""Image (mathematics)"">image</a> of a continuous function over an interval is itself an interval. .</li></ol></dd>
<dt class=""glossary"" id=""inverse_trigonometric_functions"" style=""margin-top: 0.4em;""><dfn class=""glossary""><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Inverse_trigonometric_functions"" title=""Inverse trigonometric functions"">Inverse trigonometric functions</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class=""glossary"">(Also called arcus functions,<sup id=""cite_ref-69"" class=""reference""><a href=""#cite_note-69"">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup><sup id=""cite_ref-70"" class=""reference""><a href=""#cite_note-70"">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup><sup id=""cite_ref-71"" class=""reference""><a href=""#cite_note-71"">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup><sup id=""cite_ref-72"" class=""reference""><a href=""#cite_note-72"">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup><sup id=""cite_ref-73"" class=""reference""><a href=""#cite_note-73"">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> antitrigonometric functions<sup id=""cite_ref-74"" class=""reference""><a href=""#cite_note-74"">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> or cyclometric functions<sup id=""cite_ref-75"" class=""reference""><a href=""#cite_note-75"">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup><sup id=""cite_ref-76"" class=""reference""><a href=""#cite_note-76"">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup><sup id=""cite_ref-77"" class=""reference""><a href=""#cite_note-77"">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup>) are the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Inverse_function"" title=""Inverse function"">inverse functions</a> of the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Trigonometric_functions"" title=""Trigonometric functions"">trigonometric functions</a> (with suitably restricted <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Domain_of_a_function"" title=""Domain of a function"">domains</a>). Specifically, they are the inverses of the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sine"" title=""Sine"">sine</a>, <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cosine"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Cosine"">cosine</a>, <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tangent_(trigonometry)"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Tangent (trigonometry)"">tangent</a>, <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cotangent"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Cotangent"">cotangent</a>, <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Secant_(trigonometry)"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Secant (trigonometry)"">secant</a>, and <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cosecant"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Cosecant"">cosecant</a> functions, and are used to obtain an angle from any of the angle's trigonometric ratios.</dd>

<dt class=""glossary"" id=""jump_discontinuity"" style=""margin-top: 0.4em;""><dfn class=""glossary""><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Classification_of_discontinuities#Jump_discontinuity"" title=""Classification of discontinuities"">Jump discontinuity</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class=""glossary"">Consider the function
<dl><dd><span class=""mwe-math-element""><span class=""mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y"" style=""display: none;""><math xmlns=""http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML""  alttext=""{\displaystyle f(x)={\begin{cases}x^{2}&amp;{\mbox{ for }}x&lt;1\\0&amp;{\mbox{ for }}x=1\\2-(x-1)^{2}&amp;{\mbox{ for }}x&gt;1\end{cases}}}"">
  <semantics>
    <mrow class=""MJX-TeXAtom-ORD"">
      <mstyle displaystyle=""true"" scriptlevel=""0"">
        <mi>f</mi>
        <mo stretchy=""false"">(</mo>
        <mi>x</mi>
        <mo stretchy=""false"">)</mo>
        <mo>=</mo>
        <mrow class=""MJX-TeXAtom-ORD"">
          <mrow>
            <mo>{</mo>
            <mtable columnalign=""left left"" rowspacing="".2em"" columnspacing=""1em"" displaystyle=""false"">
              <mtr>
                <mtd>
                  <msup>
                    <mi>x</mi>
                    <mrow class=""MJX-TeXAtom-ORD"">
                      <mn>2</mn>
                    </mrow>
                  </msup>
                </mtd>
                <mtd>
                  <mrow class=""MJX-TeXAtom-ORD"">
                    <mstyle displaystyle=""false"" scriptlevel=""0"">
                      <mtext>&#xA0;for&#xA0;</mtext>
                    </mstyle>
                  </mrow>
                  <mi>x</mi>
                  <mo>&lt;</mo>
                  <mn>1</mn>
                </mtd>
              </mtr>
              <mtr>
                <mtd>
                  <mn>0</mn>
                </mtd>
                <mtd>
                  <mrow class=""MJX-TeXAtom-ORD"">
                    <mstyle displaystyle=""false"" scriptlevel=""0"">
                      <mtext>&#xA0;for&#xA0;</mtext>
                    </mstyle>
                  </mrow>
                  <mi>x</mi>
                  <mo>=</mo>
                  <mn>1</mn>
                </mtd>
              </mtr>
              <mtr>
                <mtd>
                  <mn>2</mn>
                  <mo>&#x2212;<!-- − --></mo>
                  <mo stretchy=""false"">(</mo>
                  <mi>x</mi>
                  <mo>&#x2212;<!-- − --></mo>
                  <mn>1</mn>
                  <msup>
                    <mo stretchy=""false"">)</mo>
                    <mrow class=""MJX-TeXAtom-ORD"">
                      <mn>2</mn>
                    </mrow>
                  </msup>
                </mtd>
                <mtd>
                  <mrow class=""MJX-TeXAtom-ORD"">
                    <mstyle displaystyle=""false"" scriptlevel=""0"">
                      <mtext>&#xA0;for&#xA0;</mtext>

                  </mrow>

                  <mo>&gt;</mo>

            </mtable>
            <mo fence=""true"" stretchy=""true"" symmetric=""true""></mo>
          </mrow>
        </mrow>
      </mstyle>
    </mrow>
    <annotation encoding=""application/x-tex"">{\displaystyle f(x)={\begin{cases}x^{2}&amp;{\mbox{ for }}x&lt;1\\0&amp;{\mbox{ for }}x=1\\2-(x-1)^{2}&amp;{\mbox{ for }}x&gt;1\end{cases}}}</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math></span><img src=""https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/32713a375739d98e9d9258ed8eeb193419e0ec84"" class=""mwe-math-fallback-image-inline"" aria-hidden=""true"" style=""vertical-align: -3.838ex; width:34.393ex; height:8.843ex;"" alt=""f(x)={\begin{cases}x^{2}&amp;{\mbox{ for }}x&lt;1\\0&amp;{\mbox{ for }}x=1\\2-(x-1)^{2}&amp;{\mbox{ for }}x&gt;1\end{cases}}""/></span></dd></dl>

Then, the point <span class=""texhtml""><i>x</i><sub>0</sub></span> = 1 is a <i>jump discontinuity</i>.

In this case, a single limit does not exist because the one-sided limits, <span class=""texhtml""><i>L</i><sup>−</sup></span> and <span class=""texhtml""><i>L</i><sup>+</sup></span>, exist and are finite, but are not equal: since, <span class=""texhtml""><i>L</i><sup>−</sup></span> ≠ <span class=""texhtml""><i>L</i><sup>+</sup></span>, the limit <span class=""texhtml""><i>L</i></span> does not exist. Then, <span class=""texhtml""><i>x</i><sub>0</sub></span> is called a <i>jump discontinuity</i>, <i>step discontinuity</i>, or <i>discontinuity of the first kind</i>. For this type of discontinuity, the function <span class=""texhtml""><i>f</i></span> may have any value at <span class=""texhtml""><i>x</i><sub>0</sub></span>.</dd>
<dt class=""glossary"" id=""law_of_cosines"" style=""margin-top: 0.4em;""><dfn class=""glossary""><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Law_of_cosines"" title=""Law of cosines"">Law of cosines</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class=""glossary"">.</dd>
<dt class=""glossary"" id=""law_of_sines"" style=""margin-top: 0.4em;""><dfn class=""glossary""><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Law_of_sines"" title=""Law of sines"">Law of sines</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class=""glossary"">.</dd>
<dt class=""glossary"" id=""lebesgue_integration"" style=""margin-top: 0.4em;""><dfn class=""glossary""><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lebesgue_integration"" title=""Lebesgue integration"">Lebesgue integration</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class=""glossary"">In mathematics, the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Integral"" title=""Integral"">integral</a> of a non-negative <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Function_(mathematics)"" title=""Function (mathematics)"">function</a> of a single variable can be regarded, in the simplest case, as the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Area"" title=""Area"">area</a> between the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graph_of_a_function"" title=""Graph of a function"">graph</a> of that function and the <span class=""texhtml""><i>x</i></span>-axis. The <b>Lebesgue integral</b> extends the integral to a larger class of functions. It also extends the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Domain_(mathematics)"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Domain (mathematics)"">domains</a> on which these functions can be defined.</dd>
<dt class=""glossary"" id=""l&#39;hôpital&#39;s_rule"" style=""margin-top: 0.4em;""><dfn class=""glossary""><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/L%27H%C3%B4pital%27s_rule"" title=""L&#39;Hôpital&#39;s rule"">L'Hôpital's rule</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class=""glossary""><b>L'Hôpital's rule</b> or <b>L'Hospital's rule</b>  uses <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Derivative"" title=""Derivative"">derivatives</a> to help evaluate <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Limit_of_a_function"" title=""Limit of a function"">limits</a> involving <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Indeterminate_form"" title=""Indeterminate form"">indeterminate forms</a>.  Application (or repeated application) of the rule often converts an indeterminate form to an expression that can be evaluated by substitution, allowing easier evaluation of the limit. The rule is named after the 17th-century <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/France"" title=""France"">French</a> <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mathematician"" title=""Mathematician"">mathematician</a> <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Guillaume_de_l%27H%C3%B4pital"" title=""Guillaume de l&#39;Hôpital"">Guillaume de l'Hôpital</a>. Although the contribution of the rule is often attributed to L'Hôpital, the theorem was first introduced to L'Hôpital in 1694 by the Swiss mathematician <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Johann_Bernoulli"" title=""Johann Bernoulli"">Johann Bernoulli</a>.

L'Hôpital's rule states that for functions <span class=""texhtml""><var style=""padding-right: 1px;"">f</var></span> and <span class=""texhtml""><var style=""padding-right: 1px;"">g</var></span> which are <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Differentiable_function"" title=""Differentiable function"">differentiable</a> on an open <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Interval_(mathematics)"" title=""Interval (mathematics)"">interval</a> <span class=""texhtml""><var style=""padding-right: 1px;"">I</var></span> except possibly at a point <span class=""texhtml""><var style=""padding-right: 1px;"">c</var></span> contained in <span class=""texhtml""><var style=""padding-right: 1px;"">I</var></span>, if
<span class=""mwe-math-element""><span class=""mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y"" style=""display: none;""><math xmlns=""http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML""  alttext=""{\displaystyle \lim _{x\to c}f(x)=\lim _{x\to c}g(x)=0{\text{ or }}\pm \infty ,}"">
  <semantics>
