<dt class=""glossary"" id=""linear_combination"" style=""margin-top: 0.4em;""><dfn class=""glossary""><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Linear_combination"" title=""Linear combination"">Linear combination</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class=""glossary"">In <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mathematics"" title=""Mathematics"">mathematics</a>, a linear combination is an <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Expression_(mathematics)"" title=""Expression (mathematics)"">expression</a> constructed from a <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Set_(mathematics)"" title=""Set (mathematics)"">set</a> of terms by multiplying each term by a constant and adding the results (e.g. a linear combination of <i>x</i> and <i>y</i> would be any expression of the form <i>ax</i> + <i>by</i>, where <i>a</i> and <i>b</i> are constants).<sup id=""cite_ref-78"" class=""reference""><a href=""#cite_note-78"">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup><sup id=""cite_ref-79"" class=""reference""><a href=""#cite_note-79"">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup><sup id=""cite_ref-80"" class=""reference""><a href=""#cite_note-80"">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> The concept of linear combinations is central to <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Linear_algebra"" title=""Linear algebra"">linear algebra</a> and related fields of mathematics.</dd>
<dt class=""glossary"" id=""linear_equation"" style=""margin-top: 0.4em;""><dfn class=""glossary""><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Linear_equation"" title=""Linear equation"">Linear equation</a></dfn></dt>

<dt class=""glossary"" id=""multivariable_calculus"" style=""margin-top: 0.4em;""><dfn class=""glossary""><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Multivariable_calculus"" title=""Multivariable calculus"">Multivariable calculus</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class=""glossary"">.</dd>
<dt class=""glossary"" id=""natural_logarithm"" style=""margin-top: 0.4em;""><dfn class=""glossary""><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Natural_logarithm"" title=""Natural logarithm"">Natural logarithm</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class=""glossary"">The <b>natural logarithm</b> of a number is its <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Logarithm"" title=""Logarithm"">logarithm</a> to the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Base_(exponentiation)"" title=""Base (exponentiation)"">base</a> of the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mathematical_constant"" title=""Mathematical constant"">mathematical constant</a> <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/E_(mathematical_constant)"" title=""E (mathematical constant)""><i>e</i></a>, where <i>e</i> is an <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Irrational_number"" title=""Irrational number"">irrational</a> and <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Transcendental_number"" title=""Transcendental number"">transcendental</a> number approximately equal to <span class=""nowrap""><span data-sort-value=""7000271828182845899♠""></span>2.718<span style=""margin-left:.25em;"">281</span><span style=""margin-left:.25em;"">828</span><span style=""margin-left:.25em;"">459</span></span>. The natural logarithm of <i>x</i> is generally written as <span class=""nowrap"">ln <i>x</i></span>, <span class=""nowrap"">log<sub><i>e</i></sub> <i>x</i></span>,  or sometimes, if the base <i>e</i> is implicit, simply <span class=""nowrap"">log <i>x</i></span>.<sup id=""cite_ref-81"" class=""reference""><a href=""#cite_note-81"">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Parentheses"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Parentheses"">Parentheses</a> are sometimes added for clarity, giving ln(<i>x</i>), log<sub><i>e</i></sub>(<i>x</i>) or log(<i>x</i>). This is done in particular when the argument to the logarithm is not a single symbol, to prevent ambiguity.</dd>

<dt class=""glossary"" id=""numerical_integration"" style=""margin-top: 0.4em;""><dfn class=""glossary""><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Numerical_integration"" title=""Numerical integration"">Numerical integration</a></dfn></dt>

<dt class=""glossary"" id=""ordinary_differential_equation"" style=""margin-top: 0.4em;""><dfn class=""glossary""><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ordinary_differential_equation"" title=""Ordinary differential equation"">Ordinary differential equation</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class=""glossary"">.</dd>
<dt class=""glossary"" id=""pappus&#39;s_centroid_theorem"" style=""margin-top: 0.4em;""><dfn class=""glossary""><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pappus%27s_centroid_theorem"" title=""Pappus&#39;s centroid theorem"">Pappus's centroid theorem</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class=""glossary"">(Also known as  the <b>Guldinus theorem</b>, <b>Pappus–Guldinus theorem</b> or <b>Pappus's theorem</b>) is either of two related <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Theorem"" title=""Theorem"">theorems</a> dealing with the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Surface_area"" title=""Surface area"">surface areas</a> and <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Volume"" title=""Volume"">volumes</a> of <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Surface_of_revolution"" title=""Surface of revolution"">surfaces</a> and <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Solid_of_revolution"" title=""Solid of revolution"">solids</a> of revolution.</dd>
<dt class=""glossary"" id=""parabola"" style=""margin-top: 0.4em;""><dfn class=""glossary""><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Parabola"" title=""Parabola"">Parabola</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class=""glossary"">Is a <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Plane_curve"" title=""Plane curve"">plane curve</a> that is <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Reflection_symmetry"" title=""Reflection symmetry"">mirror-symmetrical</a> and is approximately U-<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shape"" title=""Shape"">shaped</a>. It fits several superficially different other <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mathematics"" title=""Mathematics"">mathematical</a> descriptions, which can all be proved to define exactly the same curves.</dd>
<dt class=""glossary"" id=""paraboloid"" style=""margin-top: 0.4em;""><dfn class=""glossary""><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Paraboloid"" title=""Paraboloid"">Paraboloid</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class=""glossary"">.</dd>

<dd class=""glossary"">.</dd>

<dt class=""glossary"" id=""piecewise-defined_function"" style=""margin-top: 0.4em;""><dfn class=""glossary""><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Piecewise"" title=""Piecewise"">Piecewise-defined function</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class=""glossary"">A function defined by multiple sub-functions that apply to certain intervals of the function's domain.</dd>

<dt class=""glossary"" id=""quadratic_function"" style=""margin-top: 0.4em;""><dfn class=""glossary""><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quadratic_function"" title=""Quadratic function"">Quadratic function</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class=""glossary"">In <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Algebra"" title=""Algebra"">algebra</a>, a <b>quadratic function</b>, a <b>quadratic polynomial</b>, a <b>polynomial of degree 2</b>, or simply a <b>quadratic</b>, is a <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polynomial_function"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Polynomial function"">polynomial function</a> with one or more variables in which the highest-degree term is of the second degree. For example, a quadratic function in three variables <i>x</i>, <i>y,</i> and <i>z</i> contains exclusively terms <i>x</i><sup>2</sup>, <i>y</i><sup>2</sup>, <i>z</i><sup>2</sup>, <i>xy</i>, <i>xz</i>, <i>yz</i>, <i>x</i>, <i>y</i>, <i>z</i>, and a constant:

<dl><dd><span class=""mwe-math-element""><span class=""mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y"" style=""display: none;""><math xmlns=""http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML""  alttext=""{\displaystyle f(x,y,z)=ax^{2}+by^{2}+cz^{2}+dxy+exz+fyz+gx+hy+iz+j,}"">
  <semantics>
    <mrow class=""MJX-TeXAtom-ORD"">

        <mo>+</mo>

</math></span><img src=""https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/27b2570145a686a65d4317f8e7e58c95213a7e39"" class=""mwe-math-fallback-image-inline"" aria-hidden=""true"" style=""vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:66.529ex; height:3.176ex;"" alt=""f(x,y,z)=ax^{2}+by^{2}+cz^{2}+dxy+exz+fyz+gx+hy+iz+j,""/></span></dd></dl>

with at least one of the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Coefficient"" title=""Coefficient"">coefficients</a> <i>a, b, c, d, e,</i> or <i>f</i> of the second-degree terms being non-zero.

A <i>univariate</i> (single-variable) quadratic function has the form<sup id=""cite_ref-wolfram_82-0"" class=""reference""><a href=""#cite_note-wolfram-82"">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup>

<dl><dd><span class=""mwe-math-element""><span class=""mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y"" style=""display: none;""><math xmlns=""http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML""  alttext=""{\displaystyle f(x)=ax^{2}+bx+c,\quad a\neq 0}"">
  <semantics>
    <mrow class=""MJX-TeXAtom-ORD"">
      <mstyle displaystyle=""true"" scriptlevel=""0"">
        <mi>f</mi>
        <mo stretchy=""false"">(</mo>
        <mi>x</mi>
        <mo stretchy=""false"">)</mo>
        <mo>=</mo>
        <mi>a</mi>
        <msup>
          <mi>x</mi>
          <mrow class=""MJX-TeXAtom-ORD"">
            <mn>2</mn>
          </mrow>
        </msup>
        <mo>+</mo>
        <mi>b</mi>
        <mi>x</mi>
        <mo>+</mo>
        <mi>c</mi>
        <mo>,</mo>
        <mspace width=""1em"" />
        <mi>a</mi>
        <mo>&#x2260;<!-- ≠ --></mo>
        <mn>0</mn>
      </mstyle>
    </mrow>
    <annotation encoding=""application/x-tex"">{\displaystyle f(x)=ax^{2}+bx+c,\quad a\neq 0}</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math></span><img src=""https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/60f6deb5a9c814c726d2185ccaca925726f564d5"" class=""mwe-math-fallback-image-inline"" aria-hidden=""true"" style=""vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:28.992ex; height:3.176ex;"" alt=""f(x)=ax^{2}+bx+c,\quad a\neq 0""/></span></dd></dl>
in the single variable <i>x</i>. The <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graph_of_a_function"" title=""Graph of a function"">graph</a> of a univariate quadratic function is a <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Parabola"" title=""Parabola"">parabola</a> whose axis of symmetry is parallel to the <span class=""texhtml""><i>y</i></span>-axis, as shown at right.

If the quadratic function is set equal to zero, then the result is a <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quadratic_equation"" title=""Quadratic equation"">quadratic equation</a>. The solutions to the univariate equation are called the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Root_of_a_function"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Root of a function"">roots</a> of the univariate function.

The bivariate case in terms of variables <i>x</i> and <i>y</i> has the form

<dl><dd><span class=""mwe-math-element""><span class=""mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y"" style=""display: none;""><math xmlns=""http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML""  alttext=""{\displaystyle f(x,y)=ax^{2}+by^{2}+cxy+dx+ey+f\,\!}"">
  <semantics>
    <mrow class=""MJX-TeXAtom-ORD"">
      <mstyle displaystyle=""true"" scriptlevel=""0"">
        <mi>f</mi>
        <mo stretchy=""false"">(</mo>
        <mi>x</mi>
        <mo>,</mo>
        <mi>y</mi>
        <mo stretchy=""false"">)</mo>
        <mo>=</mo>
        <mi>a</mi>
        <msup>
          <mi>x</mi>
          <mrow class=""MJX-TeXAtom-ORD"">
            <mn>2</mn>
          </mrow>
        </msup>
        <mo>+</mo>
        <mi>b</mi>
        <msup>
          <mi>y</mi>
          <mrow class=""MJX-TeXAtom-ORD"">
            <mn>2</mn>
          </mrow>
        </msup>
        <mo>+</mo>
        <mi>c</mi>
        <mi>x</mi>
        <mi>y</mi>
        <mo>+</mo>
        <mi>d</mi>
        <mi>x</mi>
        <mo>+</mo>
        <mi>e</mi>
        <mi>y</mi>
        <mo>+</mo>
        <mi>f</mi>
        <mspace width=""thinmathspace"" />
        <mspace width=""negativethinmathspace"" />
      </mstyle>
    </mrow>
    <annotation encoding=""application/x-tex"">{\displaystyle f(x,y)=ax^{2}+by^{2}+cxy+dx+ey+f\,\!}</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math></span><img src=""https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/cc22169897f4341363e969b77360efb72bd277ce"" class=""mwe-math-fallback-image-inline"" aria-hidden=""true"" style=""vertical-align: -0.838ex; margin-right: -0.387ex; width:40.676ex; height:3.176ex;"" alt=""f(x,y)=ax^{2}+by^{2}+cxy+dx+ey+f\,\!""/></span></dd></dl>

with at least one of <i>a, b, c</i> not equal to zero, and an equation setting this function equal to zero gives rise to a <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Conic_section"" title=""Conic section"">conic section</a> (a <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Circle"" title=""Circle"">circle</a> or other <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ellipse"" title=""Ellipse"">ellipse</a>, a <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Parabola"" title=""Parabola"">parabola</a>, or a <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hyperbola"" title=""Hyperbola"">hyperbola</a>).

In general there can be an arbitrarily large number of variables, in which case the resulting <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Surface_(geometry)"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Surface (geometry)"">surface</a> is called a <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quadric"" title=""Quadric"">quadric</a>, but the highest degree term must be of degree 2, such as <i>x</i><sup>2</sup>, <i>xy</i>, <i>yz</i>, etc.</dd>
<dt class=""glossary"" id=""quadratic_polynomial"" style=""margin-top: 0.4em;""><dfn class=""glossary""><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quadratic_polynomial"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Quadratic polynomial"">Quadratic polynomial</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class=""glossary"">.</dd>
<dt class=""glossary"" id=""quotient_rule"" style=""margin-top: 0.4em;""><dfn class=""glossary""><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quotient_rule"" title=""Quotient rule"">Quotient rule</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class=""glossary"">A formula for finding the derivative of a function that is the ratio of two functions.</dd>
<dt class=""glossary"" id=""radian"" style=""margin-top: 0.4em;""><dfn class=""glossary""><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Radian"" title=""Radian"">Radian</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class=""glossary"">Is the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_System_of_Units"" title=""International System of Units"">SI unit</a> for measuring <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Angle"" title=""Angle"">angles</a>, and is the standard unit of angular measure used in many areas of <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mathematics"" title=""Mathematics"">mathematics</a>.  The length of an arc of a <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Unit_circle"" title=""Unit circle"">unit circle</a> is numerically equal to the measurement in radians of the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Angle"" title=""Angle"">angle</a> that it <a href=""https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/subtend"" class=""extiw"" title=""wikt:subtend"">subtends</a>; one radian is just under 57.3 <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Degree_(angle)"" title=""Degree (angle)"">degrees</a> (expansion at <span class=""nowrap external""><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/On-Line_Encyclopedia_of_Integer_Sequences"" title=""On-Line Encyclopedia of Integer Sequences"">OEIS</a>:&#160;<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org//oeis.org/A072097"" class=""extiw"" title=""oeis:A072097"">A072097</a></span>). The unit was formerly an <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/SI_supplementary_unit"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""SI supplementary unit"">SI supplementary unit</a>, but this category was abolished in 1995 and the radian is now considered an <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/SI_derived_unit"" title=""SI derived unit"">SI derived unit</a>.<sup id=""cite_ref-83"" class=""reference""><a href=""#cite_note-83"">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup>  Separately, the SI unit of <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Solid_angle"" title=""Solid angle"">solid angle</a> measurement is the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Steradian"" title=""Steradian"">steradian</a> .</dd>

<dd class=""glossary"">.</dd>
